Faith and Disbelief
beginner
6 min
Truthfulness of the Heart and Arrogance
Exhortation on sincerity of heart and warning against arrogance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true truthfulness is a matter of the heart, not just the tongue, and that arrogance hardens the heart against faith.

Core Idea
Truthfulness is of the heart; arrogance is its deadly opposite.

Imam Sanusi clarifies a common misunderstanding: truthfulness is not just about what you say, but about the state of your heart. He warns that arrogance makes the heart like stone, unable to receive guidance. This lesson unpacks his powerful exhortation using Quranic verses and prophetic warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the overall message.
Notice the shift from tongue to heart.
2
Identify the definition of heart-truthfulness
Find where the author defines truthfulness of the heart. Notice what it excludes (arrogance) and why.
The definition is that the heart does not become arrogant and hardened.
3
Observe the consequences of arrogance
Look at the Quranic verses and the hadith. See how arrogance prevents hearing and leads to disbelief.
The hadith says even an atom's weight of arrogance bars Paradise.
4
Contrast with the believing heart
Read the description of the humble believer. Note how they weep upon hearing the truth and fulfill their covenant.
This is the model for us.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو وإيَّاك أن تظنَّ أنَّ الصدق مقصور على اللسان ، بل إنَّما المعني هنا صدقُ الجَنان ؛ إذ صدقُ اللسان كُلفةٌ أحياناً ، أما القلبُ فلا سبيل للكذب إليه ، فما صدقُ القلوب إذاً ؟ إنَّ صدقها ألا تتكبَّرَ فتتحجّر ، فتصير كالحجارة أو أشد قسوة ، فهي لا تريد أن تسمع ، ﴿ وَقَالَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا لَا تَسْمَعُوا هَذَا الْقُرْمَانِ وَالغَوْا فِيهِ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَغْلِبُونَ ﴾ [فصلت : ٢٦] ، بل تخافُ أن تسمع ، وَإِنِّي كُلَّمَا دَعَوْتُهُ لِتَغْفِرَ لَهُمْ جَعَلُوا أَصَبْعَهُمْ فِي مَاذَانِهِمْ وَاسْتَغْشَوْا ثِيَابَهُمْ وَأَصَرُّوا وَاسْتَكْبَرُوا اسْتِكْبَارًا [نوح : ] ، بل إن سمعَتْ ظاهراً فلن تسمع باطناً ، ولن تعي شيئاً ، وإن تَدْعُوهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى لَا يَسْمَعُوا وَتَرَنهُمْ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَيْكَ وَهُمْ لَا يُبْصِرُونَ ) [الأعراف : ١٩٨] ، قلوب خاوية لا خير فيها ، ﴿ وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لأَسْمَعَهُمْ وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوا وَهُم مُّعْرِضُونَ ) [الأنفال : ٢٣) ، وسيأتيها يوم ستسمع فيه وتبصر ولكن دونما نفع ، أَسْمِعْ بِهِمْ وَأَبْصِرْ يَوْمَ يَأْتُونَنَا لَكِن الظَّالِمُونَ الْيَوْمَ فِي ضَلَالٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ [مريم : ۳۸) . ولكون الكبر بهذا القدر من الخطر .. حذَّر منه المعلم الأكبر والهادي الأعظم عليه الصلاةُ والسلام فقال : « لا يدخل الجنَّةَ مَنْ كانَ في قلبه مثقالُ ذَرَّةٍ مِنْ كبرٍ » (۱) ، وما حجز أهل الكفر عن الإيمان إلا شجرةُ الكِبْرِ الخبيثة ، التي أنبتت عناداً وحسداً وبغضاً للعلم وأهله وأين هذه القلوبُ من قلب المؤمن الوادع اللين ، الذي يطلب الحقَّ للحقِّ ، ولا يخدع نفسَهُ بكتم العلم بعد الإحاطة به ، وكان قد عاهد الله من قبل فوفَّى بعهده ؛ لئن تبيَّنَ له أَنَّه الحقُّ ليتبعَنَّهُ ، وَإِذَا سَمِعُوا مَا أُنْزِلَ إِلَى الرَّسُولِ تَرَى أَعْيُنَهُمْ تَفِيضُ مِنَ الدَّمْعِ مِمَّا عَرَفُوا مِنَ الْحَقِّ يَقُولُونَ رَبَّنَا ءَامَنَّا فَاكْتُبْنَا مَعَ الشَّهِدِينَ ﴾ [المائدة : ۸۳] ؟ !
He said: And beware lest you think that truthfulness is restricted to the tongue, but rather the meaning here is truthfulness of the heart; for truthfulness of the tongue is sometimes a burden, but the heart has no way to lie. So what is the truthfulness of hearts? Indeed, its truthfulness is that it does not become arrogant and thus hardened, becoming like stones or even harder, so it does not want to hear. {And those who disbelieve say, "Do not listen to this Qur'an and make noise during it that you might overcome."} [Fussilat: 26], rather they fear to hear. {And whenever I call them so that You may forgive them, they put their fingers in their ears and cover themselves with their garments and persist and are greatly arrogant.} [Nuh: 7]. Moreover, even if they apparently hear, they will not hear inwardly, and will not comprehend anything. {And if you call them to guidance, they do not hear; and you see them looking at you while they do not see.} [Al-A'raf: 198]. Hearts empty, no good in them. {And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear; and if He made them hear, they would turn away while they are averse.} [Al-Anfal: 23]. And a day will come when they will hear and see, but without benefit. {How clearly they will hear and see on the Day they come to Us, but the wrongdoers today are in manifest error.} [Maryam: 38]. And because arrogance is of such danger, the greatest teacher and supreme guide, peace and blessings be upon him, warned against it, saying: 'None will enter Paradise who has the weight of an atom of arrogance in his heart.' (1) And nothing prevented the people of disbelief from faith except the evil tree of arrogance, which produced stubbornness, envy, and hatred of knowledge and its people. And where are these hearts compared to the heart of the humble, gentle believer, who seeks the truth for the truth's sake, and does not deceive himself by concealing knowledge after being informed of it, and had made a covenant with Allah before and fulfilled his covenant: if the truth becomes clear to him, he will surely follow it. {And when they hear what has been revealed to the Messenger, you see their eyes overflowing with tears because of what they recognize of the truth. They say, "Our Lord, we have believed, so write us among the witnesses."} [Al-Ma'idah: 83]? !
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is an exhortation that defines true truthfulness (sidq) and warns against arrogance (kibr). The author begins by clarifying that truthfulness is not limited to the tongue; rather, here it means truthfulness of the heart (sidq al-janan). He explains that truthfulness of the tongue can be a burden at times, but the heart cannot lie—it is either truthful or false. The truthfulness of hearts is that they do not become arrogant, which would make them hard and unable to listen. The author then supports this with several Quranic verses that describe the disbelievers' refusal to hear, their fear of hearing, and their inner deafness despite external hearing. He emphasizes that arrogance is the root cause: the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) warned that even an atom's weight of arrogance bars one from Paradise. It was only the tree of arrogance that prevented the disbelievers from faith, producing stubbornness, envy, and hatred. The passage concludes by contrasting such hearts with the humble believer who seeks truth for its own sake, does not conceal knowledge, keeps his covenant, and weeps upon hearing the truth. The structure moves from definition to scriptural evidence to prophetic warning, then to contrast.

Key Terms
الصدق
الجنان
الكبر
التكبّر
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking truthfulness is only about not lying with the tongue.
Correction: True truthfulness is primarily of the heart: being free from arrogance and open to guidance.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'truthfulness of the heart' (sidq al-janan) according to this passage?
A) Not lying with the tongue
B) Not being arrogant and thus hardened against hearing
C) Concealing knowledge
D) Making noise during Qur'an recitation

Key Takeaway
True truthfulness requires a humble heart that is open to guidance and free from arrogance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a truth from the Qur'an or Sunnah that contradicts your desires or comfort, does your heart feel a subtle resistance? That resistance is the first bud of arrogance. Will you uproot it today by humbly accepting the truth, even if it costs you?
?
The passage describes hearts that fear to hear the truth. Is there any area of your life where you avoid opening a book, attending a lecture, or having a conversation because you suspect it will challenge you? What would it mean to truly trust Allah's guidance and not your own pride?
?
Imagine yourself on the Day when you will hear and see the truth clearly but without benefit (Maryam 38). Is there any truth you are currently ignoring or minimizing? What step will you take right now to ensure your heart is soft and responsive, not like stone?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
Three Opinions on the Faith of the Muqallid
Sanusi enumerates three scholarly opinions on whether the muqallid is a true believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on whether a muqallid is a true believer and how the definition of iman underpins this disagreement.

Core Idea
The faith of a muqallid depends on the definition of iman itself.

Sanusi presents a foundational disagreement in Islamic theology: can a person who accepts religion without independent reasoning (taqlid) be considered a true believer? Different scholars have answered this based on how they define iman. Let's examine the three positions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the three opinions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the three distinct opinions Sanusi presents.
The first opinion considers nazar commendable; the second obligatory; the third considers the muqallid a disbeliever.
2
Understand the role of nazar
Notice how each opinion treats the act of reflection (nazar) differently: first as mandub, second as wajib when capable, third as essential for faith.
This shows the different weight given to independent reasoning.
3
Grasp the foundational basis
Read the part where Sanusi explains that the disagreement stems from the definition of iman. If iman is knowledge, muqallid is not believer; if acceptance, he is.
This is a usuli (foundational) issue.
4
Reflect on the attribution
Consider which schools hold each view: fuqaha' and muhaddithun for the first, some mutakallimun for the third, etc.
This helps contextualize the opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ حكم المُقلِّدِ والخلافِ فيهِ ] قوله : ( المُختلَفِ في إيمانِ صاحبِهِ )(١) الضمير يعود على ( التقليد ) ، والخلافُ الذي أشار إليه هو أَنَّهُ اختُلِفَ في إيمانِ المقلّدِ على ثلاثة أقوال (٢) : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ مؤمن غيرُ عاص بتركِ النظرِ وكأنَّ النظر عند هذا مندوب إليه فقط (٣) الثاني : أَنَّهُ مؤمن للكنَّهُ عاص إن ترك النظر معَ القدرة عليهِ ، وكأنَّ النظر عند هذا واجبٌ معَ القدرة ، ساقط مع عدمها وكأنَّ هذا يرى أنَّ المعرفة فرض على الثالثُ : أَنَّهُ كافر الأعيانِ ، وأنَّها نفْسُ الإيمان كما يقولُهُ الشيخ الأشعري ، أو لازمةٌ لهُ (١) هذا الاختلاف مسألة أصلية لا فرعية ، ومبناها الاختلاف في حقيقة الإيمان ما هو ؛ فإن فُسِّرَ بأنه حديث النفس التابع للمعرفة أو هو نفس المعرفة . . فالمقلد ليس بمؤمن ، وإن فُسِّرَ بأنه القبول للدين والتصميم عليه مطلقاً ولو من غير دليل .. فالمقلد مؤمن . « صفاقسي » ( ١/ ٣٩ ) . (٢) يعني : مشهورة ؛ فالأول : هو قول الفقهاء والمحدثين وبعض المتكلمين ، والثاني : نقله الآمدي في « أبكاره » ، والثالث : قول أبي هاشم من المعتزلة عن بعضهم ، ونسبه المصنف هنا لجمهور المتكلمين . مفاد « صفاقسي » ( ۳۹/۱) (٣) أراد : النظر الموصل إلى الدليل ، وكذا فيما سيأتي
Clarification of the ruling of the muqallid and the disagreement concerning it. His statement: 'that concerning which there is disagreement about the faith of its practitioner' – the pronoun refers to taqlid. The disagreement he pointed to is that there is a difference of opinion about the faith of the muqallid according to three statements: First: That he is a believer, not sinful for abandoning reflection, as if reflection according to this [view] is only commendable. Second: That he is a believer, but he is sinful if he abandons reflection while being capable of it, as if reflection according to this [view] is obligatory when capable, dropped when incapable, and as if this one holds that knowledge is obligatory. Third: That he is a disbeliever in essence, and that knowledge is itself faith as Shaykh al-Ash'ari says, or necessary for it. (Footnote 1) This disagreement is a foundational issue, not subordinate, and its basis is the disagreement about the reality of faith: what is it? For if it is interpreted as the inner state of the soul following knowledge, or is itself knowledge, then the muqallid is not a believer. But if it is interpreted as acceptance of the religion and determination upon it absolutely, even without evidence, then the muqallid is a believer. (From al-Safaqisi). (Footnote 2) He means: well-known; the first is the saying of the fuqaha' and muhaddithun and some mutakallimun; the second is reported by al-Amidi in his 'Abkar'; the third is the saying of Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila on the authority of some, and the author here attributes it to the majority of mutakallimun. (From al-Safaqisi). (Footnote 3) He meant: reflection that leads to evidence, and likewise in what follows.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three opinions on the faith of the muqallid (the one who practices taqlid). It begins by clarifying the subject: the disagreement is about the faith of the person who follows taqlid. Then it lists three views. First view: The muqallid is a believer and not sinful for abandoning reflection (nazar) because reflection is only commendable (mandub). Second view: The muqallid is a believer but sinful if he abandons reflection while able, because reflection is obligatory when capable but drops when incapable; knowledge is obligatory upon him. Third view: The muqallid is a disbeliever in essence, because knowledge (ma'rifa) is either identical to faith or necessary for it, as stated by Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The passage then explains that this difference is an usuli (foundational) issue, not subsidiary, and it is based on the definition of iman. If iman is defined as an inner conviction that follows knowledge or is itself knowledge, then the muqallid (who lacks independent reasoning) is not a believer. If iman is defined as acceptance of the religion and firm resolve upon it, even without evidence, then the muqallid is a believer. Footnotes attribute the first view to jurists, traditionists, and some mutakallimun; the second to al-Amidi; the third to Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila and the majority of mutakallimun. It also clarifies that 'nazar' specifically refers to reflection that leads to evidence.

Key Terms
مقلد
إيمان
نظر
معرفة
مندوب
واجب
كافر
أصلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The muqallid is automatically a believer regardless of his lack of reflection or knowledge.
Correction: This passage shows that according to some scholars, without reflection or knowledge, the muqallid may be deemed a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion on the muqallid, what is the status of reflection (nazar)?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Commendable (mandub) only
C) Sinful (haram)
D) Irrelevant to faith

Key Takeaway
The faith of a muqallid is not universally accepted; it hinges on whether iman is defined as knowledge or acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever stopped to examine why you believe in God and His Messenger? Or are you simply following what your parents taught you? The scholars disagreed on whether such a person is truly a believer. Read the three opinions again. Which one challenges you most? Commit today to studying at least one rational proof for the existence of God.
?
If faith is defined as knowledge, then what does that require from you? Are you content with a faith that is not based on evidence? This lesson shows that according to some, a muqallid may not be a believer. Look at your own heart: is your iman built on certainty or just acceptance? Will you take a step to seek knowledge and remove doubt?
?
The passage calls the disagreement about the muqallid a 'foundational issue' because it hinges on the very definition of iman. How do you define iman in your life? Is it merely a box you check, or is it a conviction that shapes your actions? This lesson forces you to ask: if you were to die tomorrow, would you be counted among the believers according to all three opinions? What needs to change?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The Three Opinions on the Faith of the Muqallid
Sanusi enumerates three opinions on the muqallid's faith and cites Imam al-Haramayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on the faith of a muqallid (one who accepts religious beliefs without personal rational inquiry) and the significance of rational inquiry (nazar) for faith.

Core Idea
Faith without inquiry? Three views debate the status of the muqallid.

Sanusi addresses a foundational question: is a person who accepts religious beliefs simply by following others (taqlid) considered a believer? The answer is not straightforward, and three major opinions exist. This lesson presents these views and their implications for the obligation of rational inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi first states the dispute and then lists three opinions with brief justifications.
The passage also includes a later quote from Imam al-Haramayn and a reference to another work.
2
Identify the three opinions
Each opinion differs on whether the muqallid is a believer and whether he is sinful for not performing nazar (rational inquiry). Note the key words: مؤمن, عاص, كافر, مندوب, واجب.
The first opinion sees nazar as commendable; the second as obligatory for those able; the third denies faith without knowledge.
3
Understand the basis of the third opinion
The third opinion is linked to the nature of faith itself: is it knowledge (ma'rifa) or inner speech? The muqallid lacks knowledge, so he is a disbeliever according to this view.
This opinion is attributed to the majority of mutakallimun by some, but others deny it.
4
Examine Imam al-Haramayn's ruling
The passage concludes with a detailed ruling: one who has sufficient time and ability for nazar but does not reflect is a disbeliever. This clarifies the practical consequence of the third opinion.
This ruling shows the importance of time and capacity in determining one's faith status.
5
Reflect on the pedagogical value
Consider why this dispute matters for theology. It highlights the role of reason in faith and the criteria for salvation.
This lesson prepares you for deeper discussions on the relationship between iman, knowledge, and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ حكم المُقلِّدِ والخلافِ فيهِ
قوله : ( المُختلَفِ في إيمانِ صاحبِهِ )(١) الضمير يعود على ( التقليد ) ، والخلافُ الذي أشار إليه هو أَنَّهُ اختُلِفَ في إيمانِ المقلّدِ على ثلاثة أقوال (۲) :
الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ مؤمن غيرُ عاص بتركِ النظرِ وكأنَّ النظر عند هذا مندوب إليه فقط (۳)
الثاني : أنَّهُ مؤمن للكنَّهُ عاص إن ترك النظرِ معَ القدرة عليهِ ، وكأنَّ النظر عند هذا واجبٌ معَ القدرة ، ساقط مع عدمها
•
وكأنَّ هذا يرى أنَّ المعرفة فرض على
الثالثُ : أَنَّهُ كافر الأعيانِ ، وأنَّها نفْسُ الإيمان كما يقولُهُ الشيخ الأشعري ، أو لازمةٌ لهُ
(۱) هذا الاختلاف مسألة أصلية لا فرعية ، ومبناها الاختلاف في حقيقة الإيمان ما هو ؛ فإن فُسِّرَ بأنه حديث النفس التابع للمعرفة أو هو نفس المعرفة . . فالمقلد ليس بمؤمن ، وإن فُسِّرَ بأنه القبول للدين والتصميم عليه مطلقاً ولو من غير دليل .. فالمقلد مؤمن . « صفاقسي » ( ١/ ٣٩ ) .
(٢) يعني : مشهورة ؛ فالأول : هو قول الفقهاء والمحدثين وبعض المتكلمين ، والثاني : نقله الآمدي في « أبكاره » ، والثالث : قول أبي هاشم من المعتزلة عن بعضهم ، ونسبه المصنف هنا لجمهور المتكلمين . مفاد « صفاقسي » ( ۳۹/۱)
(۳) أراد : النظر الموصل إلى الدليل ، وكذا فيما سيأتي

(1)
وأنَّ الإيمان هو حديثُ النفس التابع لها كما يقوله القاضي وهذا القولُ الثالثُ هو مذهب جمهورِ المُتكلّمينَ عند بعضهم وأنكرَهُ بعضُهم وقال : الجزمُ بالتقليد المطابقِ كافٍ في الإيمان ، ومخلص بفضلِ اللهِ مِنَ الخلودِ في النيرانِ ، وإنْ كانَ كثيرٌ مِنَ المحققين أنكر وجودَهُ لأهل السنَّةِ (۲) ، وبعضُهم ينقله عن أبي هاشم مِنَ المعتزلة
وقال إمام الحرمين في « شاملِهِ » : ( مَنْ ماتَ بعدَ ما مضى مِنَ الزمان ما يسعه للنظرِ ولم ينظر .. فهو كافر ، وإِنْ ماتَ قبل مُضِيَّ ما يسع ذلكَ معَ عدمِ شغلِهِ ذلكَ الزمان بما يسعه وهو مختار في ذلك .. ففي كفرِهِ قولان للقاضي ، والأصح كفرُهُ ((۳) ، وقد أشبعنا الكلام في أدلَّةِ هذا القول الثالث والرد على مَنْ خالفه في «شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، فليُنظَرْ هناك (٤) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
(۱) اقتصار المصنف في نسبة القول الثالث هنا لجمهور المتكلمين هو تغيير لما أطلق في كبراه » ، وقامت بسببه فتنة بينه وبين أهل بلده . « صفاقسي » ( ص ۳۹ ) ،
وذاك أنه جعل هذا القول لجمهور المتكلمين عند بعضهم، لا بإطلاق . وقد نُسب هذا القول للإمام الأشعري ، ونقل الإمام ابن السبكي عن القشيري جمع الجوامع ( أنه مكذوب عليه ، ثم حقَّقَ فقال : ( إن كان أخذ قول الغير بغير حجة مع احتمال شك أو وهم فلا يكفي ، وإن كان جزماً
فيكفي ) . انظر ( حاشية العطار على شرح جمع الجوامع » ( ٢/ ٤٤٥ )
(۲) في هامش ( ح ) : ( منهم الشيخ الهدهدي ) .
(۳) انظر ( المختصر الكلامي ) للإمام ابن عرفة ( ص ۱۱۲ ) .
(٤) شرح العقيدة الكبرى ( ص ١٤٧ ) .
Statement of the ruling on the muqallid and the dispute concerning it. He said: (The one disputed regarding the faith of its possessor) the pronoun refers to (taqlid), and the dispute he indicated is that there is disagreement regarding the faith of the muqallid according to three opinions: First: that he is a believer, not disobedient by abandoning reflection (nazar) — as if reflection according to this [view] is only commendable (mandub). Second: that he is a believer, but he is disobedient if he abandons reflection while having the ability for it — as if reflection according to this [view] is obligatory (wajib) when able, dropped when unable. Third: that he is a disbeliever (kafir) regarding the essentials, and that [faith] is itself faith as al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari says, or a necessary consequence of it. (1) This dispute is a principal matter, not subsidiary; its basis is the dispute over the reality of faith — what it is. For if it is interpreted as the inner speech (hadith al-nafs) following knowledge, or as knowledge itself, then the muqallid is not a believer; but if it is interpreted as acceptance of the religion and resolve upon it absolutely, even without evidence, then the muqallid is a believer. (2) Meaning: well-known; the first is the view of the jurists and hadith scholars and some mutakallimun; the second is transmitted by al-Amidi in his 'Abkar'; the third is the view of Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila, from some, and the author here attributed it to the majority of mutakallimun. (3) He intended: reflection leading to evidence, and likewise in what follows. And that faith is the inner speech (hadith al-nafs) following it as al-Qadi said — and this third opinion is the madhhab of the majority of mutakallimun according to some, and some denied it and said: Certainty (jazzm) by correct taqlid is sufficient for faith, and it saves by the grace of Allah from eternal [stay] in the Fire, although many of the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) denied its existence for the Ahl al-Sunna, and some transmit it from Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila. And Imam al-Haramayn said in his 'Shamil': 'Whoever dies after there has passed from time what would suffice for reflection and did not reflect — he is a disbeliever; and if he dies before sufficient time passes, without occupying that time with what suffices, while he is free to choose — then there are two statements from al-Qadi regarding his disbelief, and the more correct is his disbelief.' And we have thoroughly discussed the evidences for this third opinion and the refutation of those who oppose it in 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra', so let it be consulted there. And by Allah Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the three scholarly opinions regarding the faith (iman) of the muqallid — one who accepts religious beliefs by taqlid (imitation) without personal rational inquiry (nazar). The author, Sanusi, first states that the dispute concerns the ruling on the muqallid's faith. He then enumerates three views: (1) The muqallid is a believer and not sinful for abandoning nazar, because nazar is merely commendable (mandub). This is the view of many jurists and hadith scholars. (2) The muqallid is a believer but is sinful if he is able to perform nazar and does not do so, because nazar is obligatory (wajib) for those capable; it is dropped only for those incapable. (3) The muqallid is a disbeliever (kafir) with respect to the essential principles (a'yan), because true faith either is identical to knowledge (ma'rifa) — as al-Ash'ari held — or is a necessary consequence of it. The author then adds editorial notes clarifying that this third view is attributed to the majority of mutakallimun by some, but others deny it and insist that correct taqlid suffices and saves one from eternal punishment. He quotes Imam al-Haramayn's ruling: one who dies after having had sufficient time for nazar and did not engage in it is a disbeliever; if he dies before sufficient time, there is disagreement, but the more correct opinion is that he is a disbeliever. Sanusi refers readers to his larger work for detailed proofs.

Key Terms
تقليد
نظر
إيمان
مقلد
معرفة
مندوب
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third opinion declares all muqallids disbelievers without exception.
Correction: The third opinion applies only to those capable of nazar and with sufficient time; Imam al-Haramayn's ruling shows that capacity and time matter.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion on the muqallid's faith, what is the ruling on nazar (rational inquiry)?
A) It is commendable (mandub) for those able.
B) It is obligatory (wajib) for those able, and dropped for those unable.
C) It is obligatory for everyone regardless of ability.
D) It is not required at all.

Key Takeaway
The faith of a muqallid is disputed; the core issue is whether true faith requires personal rational inquiry or can be based on accepted belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever critically examined the foundations of your own faith, or are you content to rely on what you were taught as a child? If you were to die today, could you honestly say your belief is based on genuine knowledge or mere imitation?
?
If rational inquiry into the principles of faith were obligatory and neglect of it could render one a disbeliever, how would that change the urgency with which you pursue knowledge of your Creator? Are you willing to risk eternal punishment for the comfort of uncritical acceptance?
?
When you defend your beliefs to others, do you rely on arguments you have truly understood and verified, or do you repeat what you have heard without personal conviction? What would it take for you to move from taqlid to certainty?

The Third Opinion on Faith and the Obligation of Rational Inquiry
Sanusi discusses the third opinion on iman and the ruling on those who neglect nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion on the definition of faith (iman) and the ruling on those who neglect rational inquiry (nazar) according to Sanusi and Imam al-Haramayn.

Core Idea
Faith is inner speech; neglecting rational inquiry when capable leads to unbelief.

This passage presents a nuanced discussion on the nature of faith and the role of rational inquiry. Sanusi introduces a third opinion that defines faith as the speech of the soul, then addresses the obligation of nazar and the consequences of neglecting it, quoting Imam al-Haramayn. The lesson clarifies these key points and their implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: a definition, a contrasting view, and a ruling.
The passage references other scholars and a larger work; focus on the main points.
2
Identify the third opinion on iman
Find where Sanusi states that faith is 'speech of the soul' (hadith al-nafs). Understand that this opinion is attributed to al-Qadi and the majority of theologians according to some.
Also note that some deny this and hold that certainty through taqlid is sufficient.
3
Understand the ruling on neglecting nazar
Examine the quote from Imam al-Haramayn. Distinguish between the two scenarios: dying after sufficient time for nazar (clear kufr) and dying before sufficient time passes with negligence (two opinions, stronger view is kufr).
This connects to the broader debate on the necessity of rational inquiry for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنَّ الإيمان هو حديثُ النفس التابع لها كما يقوله القاضي وهذا القولُ الثالثُ هو مذهب جمهورِ المُتكلّمينَ عند بعضهم وأنكرَهُ بعضُهم وقال : الجزمُ بالتقليد المطابقِ كافٍ في الإيمان ، ومخلص بفضلِ اللهِ مِنَ الخلودِ في النيرانِ ، وإنْ كانَ كثيرٌ مِنَ المحققين أنكر وجودَهُ لأهل السنَّةِ (۲) ، وبعضُهم ينقله عن أبي هاشم مِنَ المعتزلة
وقال إمام الحرمين في « شاملِهِ » : ( مَنْ ماتَ بعدَ ما مضى مِنَ الزمان ما يسعه للنظرِ ولم ينظر .. فهو كافر ، وإِنْ ماتَ قبل مُضِيَّ ما يسع ذلكَ معَ عدمِ شغلِهِ ذلكَ الزمان بما يسعه وهو مختار في ذلك .. ففي كفرِهِ قولان للقاضي ، والأصح كفرُهُ ((۳) ، وقد أشبعنا الكلام في أدلَّةِ هذا القول الثالث والرد على مَنْ خالفه في «شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، فليُنظَرْ هناك (٤) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
And that faith is the speech of the soul (ḥadīth al-nafs) following it, as al-Qāḍī says. And this third opinion is the way of the majority of the theologians (jumhūr al-mutakallimīn) according to some of them, and some of them denied it and said: Certainty (al-jazm) through blind following (al-taqlīd) that corresponds (al-muṭābiq) is sufficient for faith and saves, by the grace of God, from eternal damnation, although many of the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqīn) denied its existence for the Ahl al-Sunnah, and some of them transmit it from Abū Hāshim of the Mu‘tazila. And Imām al-Ḥaramayn said in his ‘Shāmil’: ‘Whoever dies after enough time has passed for him to engage in rational inquiry (al-naẓar) and did not engage in it – he is an unbeliever (kāfir). And if he dies before the passing of that time sufficient for it, without having occupied that time with what would be sufficient for it, while he had the choice in that – then regarding his unbelief there are two opinions from al-Qāḍī, and the more correct is his unbelief.’ And we have thoroughly discussed the proofs of this third opinion and the refutation of those who oppose it in ‘Sharḥ al-‘Aqīda al-Kubrā’, so let one look there. And with God Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents a theological discussion on the nature of faith (īmān) and the role of taqlīd (blind following) versus naẓar (rational inquiry). The author first states a third opinion on the definition of īmān: that it is the inner speech of the soul (ḥadīth al-nafs) that follows the person's conviction, as held by al-Qāḍī. This opinion is attributed to the majority of theologians according to some, but others deny it and assert that certainty (jazm) through taqlīd, when it corresponds to the truth, is sufficient for saving faith, though many verifiers reject that this opinion actually exists among Ahl al-Sunnah, and some trace it to the Mu‘tazilī Abū Hāshim. The passage then quotes Imām al-Ḥaramayn from his work ‘al-Shāmil’, who delivers a ruling on those who neglect naẓar. He states that if a person dies after sufficient time has passed for them to engage in rational inquiry and they did not do so, they are an unbeliever. If they die before that time has passed, without having occupied themselves with what would suffice, while having the choice, then there are two opinions from al-Qāḍī, but the more correct view is that they are also an unbeliever. Finally, the author notes that a fuller treatment of this third opinion and its proofs is found in his commentary ‘Sharḥ al-‘Aqīda al-Kubrā’.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any form of taqlid is sufficient for faith regardless of time and capability.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between taqlid that corresponds to truth and the obligation of nazar. Imam al-Haramayn rules that neglecting nazar when capable leads to kufr.

Checkpoint

A) He is a believer
B) He is an unbeliever
C) There are two opinions
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The third opinion defines faith as inner speech, and neglecting rational inquiry when capable is considered unbelief according to Imam al-Haramayn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever deliberately avoided questioning your beliefs because it felt uncomfortable? This passage teaches that neglecting rational inquiry when you have the ability and time can lead to kufr. Will you commit today to setting aside time to learn and intellectually engage with the proofs of your faith?
?
If faith is truly the inner speech of your soul, what does your inner voice say about your relationship with Allah right now? Are you content with blindly repeating what others say, or have you personally verified the foundations of your belief?
?
Imam al-Haramayn says that if you die after having had enough time to reflect on your faith but didn't, you are a disbeliever. Have you honestly accounted for the time you've been given? What concrete step will you take this week to fulfill the obligation of nazar?

Ibn Dahaq on the Trial of the Grave and the Two Types of Hypocrisy
Ibn Dahaq warns against taqlid and distinguishes two types of nifaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why blind following (taqlid) without rational inquiry (nazar) leads to hidden hypocrisy that fails the trial of the grave.

Core Idea
Blind faith without reflection is a hidden hypocrisy.

Ibn Dahaq opens a serious warning: the trial of the grave is not escaped by those who simply follow their family's religion without thinking. He then reveals a subtle but dangerous kind of hypocrisy—one that its owner is completely unaware of. This passage calls us to examine the foundation of our faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage ends mid-sentence; notice the gravity of the warning.
2
Identify the two types of nifaq
The passage explicitly distinguishes two types of hypocrisy. Find the description of each and note how they differ, especially what the person knows about their own state.
The second type is dangerous because the person is unaware.
3
Note the role of the devil and reflection
Ibn Dahaq describes how a devil discourages reflection by claiming it leads to doubt. See how this prevents the person from escaping taqlid.
This connects to the idea that nazar is essential for sound faith.
4
Reflect on your own faith
Ask yourself: do I hold my beliefs because of upbringing or after genuine inquiry? The passage suggests that only the latter will survive the grave's trial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالَ ابنُ دهاق رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى في « شرح الإرشادِ » لما تكلَّم على فتنة القبر وعذابِهِ (۱) ؛ قالَ : ( وهذه الفتنةُ فتنةُ القبرِ لا ينجو منها مَنْ أخذَ في دينِهِ بالتقليدِ ، وترك النظر في أدلَّةِ الرسالة والتوحيدِ ، ولذلكَ قيلَ : النفاقُ نفاقانِ : نفاقُ يعرفُهُ صاحبُهُ مِنْ نفسِهِ ، ونفاقٌ لا يعرفُهُ مِنْ نفسه . فالأول : هو نفاقُ الذين كانوا يعبدون الأصنام خُفْيَةً ، ويُظهرونَ معالم الدين ؛ كالقول والصلاة . وأما النفاقُ الذي لا يشعرُ به صاحبُهُ : فهو أن يُولَدَ الرجلُ أو المرأةُ بين أبوين مؤمنين ، فيسمع قول : لا إله إلا الله ، محمدٌ رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، فيقول نحو ما يسمعُ اتباعاً وتقليداً لهم ، حتى إنَّهُ لو وُلِدَ بين النصارى لقال بأقوالهم اتِّباعاً لهم وتقليداً ، مِنْ غير أن ينظر في خلقِهِ ، ومِنْ أي شيءٍ خُلِقَ ، وكيف انتقلَ مِنْ طور إلى طورٍ . وربَّما يمرُّ بباله الفكرُ في خَلْقِ اللهِ تعالى ، فيردُّه شيطانُ مِنَ الإِنسِ أوِ الجنّ فيقولُ لهُ : إِنْ تفكَّرْتَ فقد تشكَّكْتَ ، فيُعرِضُ عَنِ النظرِ حتى إلى الموتِ (۲) ، فإذا بلغَتِ الروحُ الحُلْقُومَ أَتاه الشيطانُ في ذلكَ
Ibn Dahaq – may God have mercy on him – said in ‘Sharḥ al-Irshād’ when he discussed the trial of the grave and its punishment: (And this trial – the trial of the grave – no one escapes it who has taken his religion by blind following (taqlīd) and abandoned rational inquiry (naẓar) into the proofs of the message and of tawḥīd. For that reason it is said: Hypocrisy (nifāq) is of two types: hypocrisy that its owner knows from himself, and hypocrisy that he does not know from himself. The first: it is the hypocrisy of those who used to worship idols secretly and manifest the signs of the religion, such as speech and prayer. As for the hypocrisy that its owner does not perceive: it is that a man or a woman is born to believing parents, and hears the statement: ‘There is no god but God, Muḥammad is the Messenger of God – may God bless him and grant him peace – and he says something similar to what he hears, following and imitating them, such that if he had been born among Christians he would have uttered their statements following and imitating them, without reflecting upon his own creation, from what thing he was created, and how he moved from one stage to another. And perhaps a thought about the creation of God Almighty passes his mind, but a devil from among humans or jinn repels him and says to him: ‘If you reflect, you will become doubtful,’ so he turns away from reflection until death. Then when the soul reaches the throat, the devil comes to him at that time.)
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a warning from Ibn Dahaq about the danger of taqlīd (blind following) in relation to the trial of the grave (fitnat al-qabr). It begins by stating that no one who has taken his religion through taqlīd and abandoned naẓar (rational inquiry) into the proofs of prophethood and tawḥīd will escape the trial of the grave. The reason is that such a person’s faith is not firmly rooted. Ibn Dahaq then cites the observation that hypocrisy (nifāq) is of two types. The first type is conscious hypocrisy: the person knows he is being hypocritical, like those who secretly worshipped idols while outwardly practicing Islam. The second type is hidden hypocrisy: the person is unaware of it. This occurs when someone is born to Muslim parents and simply repeats the shahāda by imitation, without personal reflection. If he had been born to Christians, he would have followed their beliefs similarly. He does not contemplate his own creation, his origins, or his development through stages. If a thought about God’s creation occurs to him, a devil (from humans or jinn) discourages him, saying it would lead to doubt. He thus refrains from reflection until death. At the moment of death, when the soul reaches the throat, the devil comes to him, presumably to make his faith waver. The passage is cut off at this point.

تقليد
نظر
نفاق
شيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being born into a Muslim family guarantees true faith that will save one in the grave.
Correction: This passage shows that mere upbringing without personal reflection and inquiry constitutes a hidden hypocrisy that will be exposed in the grave, leading to failure.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Dahaq, what happens when a thought about God's creation passes the mind of a person relying on taqlid?
A) He immediately becomes a believer
B) A devil discourages him from reflecting
C) He naturally starts to reflect deeply
D) He is saved from hypocrisy

Key Takeaway
Authentic faith requires personal inquiry; mere imitation may conceal a hidden hypocrisy that will be exposed in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever honestly asked yourself: 'If I were born into a non-Muslim family, would I still be Muslim today?' This lesson warns that mere imitation of our parents is a hidden hypocrisy. What would you say to Allah when He asks why you believed?
?
When a thought about the creation of the universe or your own existence crosses your mind, do you quickly dismiss it, fearing that thinking might lead to doubt? The passage says a devil discourages such reflection. Are you afraid to think deeply about your faith?
?
At the moment of death, your faith will be tested. If your belief is merely inherited and never personally examined, how will you respond when the devil comes to shake your certainty? What one concrete step can you take today to build a conviction that is truly your own?

The Danger of Taqlid and Two Types of Hypocrisy
Ibn Duhaq warns against taqlid and defines two types of hypocrisy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid (blind imitation) is insufficient for salvation and how Ibn Duhaq classifies hypocrisy into two types, one of which is hidden from the person themselves.

Core Idea
Taqlid without proof leads to hidden hypocrisy that destroys faith at death.

Ibn Duhaq opens by declaring that the trial of the grave cannot be escaped by those who merely imitate their religion without evidence. He then introduces a striking distinction between two kinds of hypocrisy—one intentional and known, the other unintentional and hidden. This passage forces us to examine whether our faith is based on genuine conviction or mere habit.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening warning
Read from the beginning: 'لا ينجو منها مَنْ أخذَ في دينِهِ بالتقليدِ...' Notice the absolute link between taqlid and failure in the grave trial.
The word 'نجو' means escape; only those with proof-based knowledge can escape.
2
Identify the two types of hypocrisy
The first type is known to its owner—outwardly Muslim, inwardly idol-worshipper. The second is hidden—born into a Muslim family, merely repeating the shahada without reflection.
The second type is more dangerous because the person is unaware of their hypocrisy.
3
Reflect on the condition of the second type
Ibn Duhaq says such a person would have professed Christianity if born among Christians. This shows that environment-based faith lacks true conviction.
This highlights the importance of personal reflection (nazar).

قالَ ابنُ دهاق رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى في « شرح الإرشادِ » لما تكلَّم على فتنة القبر وعذابِهِ قالَ : ( وهذه الفتنةُ فتنةُ القبرِ لا ينجو منها مَنْ أخذَ في دينِهِ بالتقليدِ ، وترك النظر في أدلَّةِ الرسالة والتوحيدِ ، ولذلكَ قيلَ : النفاقُ نفاقانِ : نفاقُ يعرفُهُ صاحبُهُ مِنْ نفسِهِ ، ونفاقٌ لا يعرفُهُ مِنْ نفسه . فالأول : هو نفاقُ الذين كانوا يعبدون الأصنام خُفْيَةً ، ويُظهرونَ معالم الدين ؛ كالقول والصلاة . وأما النفاقُ الذي لا يشعرُ به صاحبُهُ : فهو أن يُولَدَ الرجلُ أو المرأةُ بين أبوين مؤمنين ، فيسمع قول : لا إله إلا الله ، محمدٌ رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، فيقول نحو ما يسمع اتباعاً وتقليداً لهم ، حتى إنَّهُ لو وُلِدَ بين النصارى لقال بأقوالهم اتِّباعاً لهم وتقليداً ، مِنْ غير أن ينظر في خلقِهِ ، ومِنْ أي شيءٍ خُلِقَ ، وكيف انتقلَ مِنْ طور إلى طورٍ . وربَّما يمرُّ بباله الفكرُ في خَلْقِ اللهِ تعالى ، فيردُّه شيطانُ مِنَ الإِنسِ أوِ الجنّ فيقولُ لهُ : إِنْ تفكَّرْتَ فقد تشكَّكْتَ ، فيُعرِضُ عَنِ النظرِ حتى إلى الموتِ ، فإذا بلغَتِ الروحُ الحُلْقُومَ أَتاه الشيطانُ في ذلك المضيق حينَ لا فكر ، فيُشكِّكُهُ في دينِهِ ، فيموتُ على شكّهِ والعياذُ بالله تعالى . وإذا كانَ في القبرِ خُتِمَ على الأفواه ، ونطق بما عندَهُ ؛ فإِنْ كانَ عارفاً نطق بالحقِّ ، وإنْ كانَ شاكاً غير عالم قال : لا أدري ، وكذا كانَ في حياتِهِ يقولُ بقلبِهِ : لا أدري ، وكانَ يطرقه الشكُ أحياناً ولا يبحث عليه ، ولا يداوي سَقام سريرته ، فإذا مات لحقه الندم حينَ لا ينفعه ، واعتذرَ إلى مَنْ لا يسمعُهُ ، وهلك والعياذُ باللهِ مِنْ سخط اللهِ تعالى
Ibn Duhaq, may Allah have mercy on him, said in 'Sharh al-Irshad' when he spoke about the trial of the grave and its punishment: (This trial, the trial of the grave, none escapes it who takes his religion by taqlid (blind imitation) and abandons reflection on the proofs of the message and tawhid. Therefore it was said: Hypocrisy is of two types: hypocrisy that its owner knows from himself, and hypocrisy that he does not know from himself. The first is the hypocrisy of those who used to worship idols secretly and manifest the outward signs of the religion, like speech and prayer. As for the hypocrisy that its owner does not perceive: it is that a man or woman is born between two believing parents, and hears the statement: La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammad Rasul Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and he says something similar to what he hears, following and imitating them, so much so that if he had been born among Christians he would have said their statements, following and imitating them, without reflecting on his own creation, and from what thing he was created, and how he moved from one stage to another. And perhaps a thought about the creation of Allah the Exalted passes through his mind, but a devil from humans or jinn repels him, saying: If you reflect, you will doubt. So he turns away from reflection until death. When the soul reaches the throat, the devil comes to him in that narrow strait when there is no thought, and causes him to doubt his religion, and he dies upon his doubt, and refuge is sought with Allah the Exalted. And when he is in the grave, mouths are sealed, and he speaks of what he has. If he is a knower, he speaks the truth; if he is a doubter, not knowing, he says: I do not know. And similarly during his life he used to say in his heart: I do not know, and doubt would strike him sometimes and he would not investigate it, nor treat the sickness of his inner state. When he dies, regret overtakes him when it is of no use, and he apologizes to one who does not hear him, and he perishes, and refuge is sought with Allah, from the wrath of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a warning from Ibn Duhaq about the danger of taqlid (blind imitation) in religious matters, specifically in the context of the trial of the grave. He begins by stating that the trial of the grave is inescapable for anyone who adopts his religion purely through taqlid and abandons the use of rational proofs (al-nazar fi adillat al-risala wa al-tawhid). This establishes the central thesis: salvation from the grave's trial depends on genuine knowledge founded on evidence, not mere rote acceptance.

Ibn Duhaq then introduces a classification of hypocrisy (nifaq) into two types. The first type is known by the person himself—that is, the hypocrites who outwardly displayed Islamic rites like prayer while inwardly worshipping idols. This was the hypocrisy of the munafiqun in Medina. The second type is hypocrisy of which the person is unaware, and this is the main focus. It describes a person born into a Muslim family who merely repeats the shahada and other Islamic statements out of imitation of his parents, without any personal reflection or investigation. Such a person would have done the same if born among Christians. He does not ponder his own creation or the stages of his life. If a thought about Allah's creation occurs to him, a devil (human or jinn) dissuades him by saying that reflection will lead to doubt, so he abandons reflection altogether.

As death approaches, when the soul reaches the throat, the devil comes to him in that narrow moment when thought is no longer possible, and causes him to doubt his religion. He dies in a state of doubt. In the grave, when mouths are sealed and the angels question him, if he is a true knower he answers with truth; but if he is a doubter and ignorant, he says 'I do not know.' This reflects his lifelong inner state: he used to say 'I do not know' in his heart, and he would be struck by doubt but never investigated it or cured the sickness of his inner state. After death, he feels regret when it is useless, and he perishes under Allah's wrath.

Thus, Ibn Duhaq uses this vivid scenario to demonstrate that taqlid without verification leads to doubt at the critical moment, and only certain knowledge (ma'rifa) based on proof (burhan) can ensure a successful answer in the grave trial.

Key Terms
تقليد
نفاق
فتنة القبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that being born into a Muslim family guarantees salvation.
Correction: Ibn Duhaq warns that mere birth and imitation without personal conviction does not protect from doubt at death and punishment in the grave.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Duhaq, what distinguishes the second type of hypocrisy from the first?
A) The person is aware of his hypocrisy.
B) The person is unaware of his hypocrisy because he merely imitates his parents without reflection.
C) It involves worship of idols.
D) It is only found among Christians.

Key Takeaway
True faith requires personal reflection on proofs, not mere imitation of family or environment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say the shahada, is it a conviction based on your own investigation of the proofs, or is it merely the echo of what you heard growing up? Would your heart remain Muslim if you had been born in a Christian home?
?
Ibn Duhaq says that the one who does taqlid does not reflect on his own creation. When was the last time you actually pondered over how you were created from a drop, then clot, then bones, then flesh? Do you feel the weight of that creation or just take it for granted?
?
The trial of the grave is real and inescapable for those who merely imitate. If you were to die tonight, would your faith in the grave be that of a convinced believer or an imitator? What one step will you take today to move from imitation to personal certainty?

The Process of Doubt and the Grave Trial
Ibn Duhaq describes the inner journey of the hidden hypocrite from doubt to death and the grave trial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how doubt arises from suppressing reflection, how it leads to death on doubt, and how the grave trial reveals the inner state of the person.

Core Idea
A lifetime of ignoring doubts results in a final answer of 'I do not know' in the grave.

Ibn Duhaq now traces the psychological and spiritual path of the hidden hypocrite. A thought about creation is planted, but a devil—human or jinn—warns that reflection leads to doubt, so the person abandons it. This sets the stage for a deathbed crisis and the final exposure in the grave. This lesson shows the real-world consequences of taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Follow the suppression of reflection
Read how a thought about Allah's creation arises but is suppressed by a devil's warning: 'If you reflect, you will doubt.' The person turns away from reflection.
This shows that the fear of doubt prevents the person from reaching certainty.
2
Observe the deathbed crisis
When the soul reaches the throat, the devil returns and causes doubt in a moment when thought is impossible. The person dies on doubt.
The phrase 'حينَ لا فكر' (when there is no thought) indicates the vulnerability at death.
3
Examine the grave trial
In the grave, the person's mouth is sealed and speaks according to his inner state. If a knower, he says truth; if a doubter, he says 'I do not know'.
This reflects his lifelong inner state of uncertainty that he never treated.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Faith Requires Knowledge, Not Blind Imitation
Sanusi proves faith requires knowledge using Quranic parables and logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why faith must be based on knowledge and evidence, using Quranic parables and the argument that faith and disbelief cannot be equal in lacking proof.

Core Idea
Faith is not mere imitation; it is rooted in knowledge and evidence.

In this passage, Sanusi uses powerful Quranic imagery and logical argument to establish that true faith requires certainty and insight, not blind acceptance. He contrasts the rooted tree of faith with the uprooted tree of disbelief, and argues that if faith were without evidence, it would be equivalent to disbelief—a clear impossibility. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the Quranic quotations embedded in the argument.
2
Identify the Quranic parable
Find the parable of the good tree and bad tree. Understand how Sanusi interprets them as faith and disbelief.
The good tree has firm roots; the bad tree is uprooted.
3
Understand the logical argument
Sanusi argues that if faith were without evidence, it would equal disbelief (which also lacks evidence). This is impossible because the Quran distinguishes them.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the necessity of reflection
The passage concludes with the command to reflect. Why is intellectual effort (nazar) essential for knowledge of Allah?
Reflection leads to knowledge of what is obligatory, permissible, and impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حصولُ اليقين بإيمانِهِ ، والتثبيتُ في الآخرة لا معنى له إلا النطقُ على نحو ما يعرفُه (١) ؛ لأنَّ العبد على ما مات عليهِ يُبْعَثُ (٢)
وقد ضربَ الله العظيم لذلكَ مثلاً فقالَ : ضَرَبَ اللهُ مَثَلًا كَلِمَةً طَيِّبَةً كَشَجَرَةِ طَيِّبَةٍ أَصْلُهَا ثَابِتُ وَفَرْعُهَا فِي السَّمَاء ) [إبراهيم : ٢٤] قال أهلُ الحقِّ : الكلمة الطيبةُ : لا إله إلا الله ، محمدٌ رسولُ اللهِ أصلُها ثابت في القلب ، وهي مسموعة في اللسان ، وهي التي تصعد بسببها الأعمال ، وتصلح بها الأحوال
وقال تعالى : ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ كَشَجَرَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ أُجْتُنَّتْ مِن فَوْقِ الْأَرْضِ مَا لَهَا مِن قَرَارِ) [إبراهيم : (٢٦) ، وهي كلمة الكفر ، لا ثبوت لها في القلب ؛ أي : لا علم لصاحبها بحقيقتها ؛ إذ الكفرُ لا دليل عليهِ وإنَّما يحصل عن تقليد مِنْ غيرِ برهان ، ﴿ وَمَن يَدْعُ مَعَ اللَّهِ إِلَنهَا آخَرَ لَا بُرْهَانَ لَم بِهِ (٣) [المؤمنون : ۱۱۷] ، فالكفر لا برهان عليه ، فلو كانَ الإيمانُ أيضاً كذلك لكانا في حكم التسوية في عدم البرهان واحداً ! والقرآن العظيم شاهدٌ بأنَّ دينَ اللهِ أهلُه على علم وبصيرةٍ مِنْ أمرِه ، قال تعالى : ﴿ قُلْ هَذِهِ سَبِيلِي أَدْعُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ عَلَى بَصِيرَةٍ أَنَا وَمَنِ اتَّبَعَنِي ) يوسف : ۱۰۸] ، فقوله تعالى : ﴿ عَلَى بَصِيرَة ) أي : على علم ودلالة دونَ أخذ بالظنون في طُرُقِ التقليدِ ؛ فالمؤمنُ على علم بدينه ، وبصيرة من يقينِهِ ، والكافرُ على ضلالةٍ في عَمَهِ وحَيرة ، لا يهتدي إلى الحقِّ سبيلاً ، ولا يعرفُ برهاناً ولا دليلاً ، وَمَن كَانَ فِي هَذِهِ أَعْمَىٰ فَهُوَ فِي ا الآخِرَةِ أَعْمَى وَأَضَلُّ سَبِيلاً) [الإسراء : ٧٢] .
وأوامر الله سبحانه وتعالى بالنظرِ في كتابه العزيز لا تُعَدُّ كثرةً ، وليس في ذلكَ مِنْ معرفةِ الحكمةِ إلا أنَّ الفكرَ يُبلِّغُ (١) إلى العلم بما يجبُ اللهِ وما يجوز له وما يستحيل عليه ، ولا سبيل إلى العلم بذلك إلا بالنظر ، وقد ذكرنا ذلك في خُطبة هذا المجموع (٢) ، فأغنى ذلك عنْ إعادته
Attaining certainty by his faith, and confirmation in the afterlife, has no meaning except utterance in the manner that he knows (1); because the servant is resurrected upon what he died upon (2). And Allah Almighty struck for that an example, saying: "Allah presents an example: a good word like a good tree, its root is firm and its branch is in the sky" [Ibrahim: 24]. The people of truth said: "The good word is 'la ilaha illa Allah, Muhammadun Rasul Allah'; its root is firm in the heart, it is heard on the tongue, and it is by it that deeds ascend and conditions are set aright." And He Almighty said: "And the example of a bad word is like a bad tree, uprooted from above the earth, having no stability" [Ibrahim: 26]. It is the word of disbelief; it has no stability in the heart, meaning: its possessor has no knowledge of its reality; because disbelief has no evidence, rather it occurs through blind imitation without proof. "And whoever invokes besides Allah another deity for which he has no evidence" [Al-Mu'minun: 117]. So disbelief has no evidence. If faith also were like that, then they would be in a ruling of equivalence, being one in lack of evidence! And the Great Quran bears witness that the religion of Allah, its people are upon knowledge and insight from His command. He Almighty said: "Say: 'This is my way; I invite to Allah with insight, I and those who follow me'" [Yusuf: 108]. So His Almighty saying: "with insight" means: upon knowledge and indication, without taking by conjecture in the ways of blind imitation. So the believer is upon knowledge of his religion, and insight from his certainty, and the disbeliever is upon misguidance in his blindness and confusion, he does not find guidance to the truth, nor does he know proof or evidence. "And whoever is blind in this [world] will be blind in the Hereafter and even more astray from the path" [Al-Isra: 72]. And the commands of Allah Almighty to reflect in His Noble Book are countless, and in that there is no [need for] understanding the wisdom except that thought conveys (1) to knowledge of what is obligatory for Allah, what is permissible for Him, and what is impossible for Him. And there is no way to knowledge of that except through reflection. And we have mentioned that in the introduction of this collection (2), so that suffices for repeating it.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a proof that faith must be based on knowledge and evidence, not blind imitation. It begins by stating that the certainty of faith and its confirmation in the afterlife only have meaning if the utterance of faith (shahada) is accompanied by knowledge of its meaning. Then it invokes the Quranic parable of the good tree (rooted) and the bad tree (uprooted). The good word is the shahada, firmly rooted in the heart, leading to righteous deeds. The bad word is disbelief, which has no stability in the heart because its follower has no true knowledge of it. Disbelief has no evidence; it arises from blind imitation (taqlid) without proof (burhan). The author then argues that if faith were also without evidence, then faith and disbelief would be equal in lacking proof—which is impossible given the Quran's testimony. He cites the verse "Invite to Allah with insight" to show that the Prophet and his followers call to Allah based on knowledge, not conjecture. The believer has knowledge and certainty, while the disbeliever is in confusion and cannot find proof. Finally, he notes that Allah's commands to reflect in the Quran are numerous, and the purpose of reflection is to attain knowledge of what is obligatory, permissible, and impossible for Allah—and this knowledge can only be reached through intellectual reflection (nazar). This establishes that faith requires intellectual effort, not mere imitation.

إيمان
كلمة طيبة
كلمة خبيثة

بصيرة

برهان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that faith is simply saying the shahada without understanding or evidence.
Correction: This passage shows faith requires knowledge and proof, as disbelief lacks evidence and faith cannot be equivalent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is disbelief likened to a bad tree?
A) It has no root in the heart because it lacks knowledge and evidence.
B) It is harmful to society.
C) It leads to bad deeds.
D) It is temporary.

Key Takeaway
Faith must be based on knowledge and evidence, not blind imitation; the Quranic parables and logical reasoning affirm this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you know why you believe in Allah? Can you present a single clear proof for His existence from the Quran or intellect? If not, are you living in the blind imitation that this passage equates with disbelief?
?
Your shahada: is it firmly rooted in your heart with understanding, or merely on your tongue as a habit? What will you do today to move from inherited faith to personal certainty?
?
The Prophet called to Allah 'with insight.' Are you content to follow without seeking insight? What is one specific question about your faith that you have never bothered to answer, and when will you investigate it?

Refutation of Taqlid and Two Types of Belief
Sanusi reports rejections of taqlid and introduces al-Sharif Abu Yahya's classification of belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid is rejected in creedal matters and be introduced to a classification of belief into two types.

Core Idea
Faith requires proof for every pillar; belief is of two types.

This passage continues the condemnation of blind imitation in matters of faith. It cites major scholars affirming that genuine faith must be based on rational proof, and introduces a twofold classification of belief by al-Sharif Abu Yahya. Understanding these points solidifies the principle that taqlid is insufficient for true iman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the authorities cited
Read the passage and note the names: al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq, Ibn al-Qassar, Imam Malik, and al-Sharif Abu Yahya. Each contributes to the argument against taqlid.
These are major scholars in the tradition.
2
Grasp the key principle
The muhaqqiqun (verifying scholars) state that no one has faith unless they have a proof (dalil) for every pillar of religion. This is the core doctrinal point.
Faith here means genuine iman, not mere affirmation.
3
Distinguish positions on taqlid
Only ahl al-zahir (people of apparent meaning) accept taqlid, while scholars like Malik require reflection (nazar). Recognize that taqlid is insufficient for creed.
This aligns with the broader kalam emphasis on rational evidence.
4
Observe the introduction of two types
Al-Sharif Abu Yahya begins to classify belief into two types. Though the passage cuts off, this signals a further elaboration on the nature of belief.
The classification is explored in subsequent lessons.

The Critique of Taqlid in Matters of Belief
Sanusi critiques Sharif's classification of i'tiqad regarding taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid (blind following) in matters of belief is insufficient and how scholars argue for the obligation of independent reasoning (nazar).

Core Idea
Belief without evidence is not acceptable; every rational person must use nazar for the fundamentals of faith.

In this passage, Imam al-Sanusi addresses a critical debate in Islamic theology: Can a person be considered a true believer if they simply follow others without personal conviction? He critiques the view of al-Sharif Abu Yahya, who attempted to excuse taqlid for those incapable of reasoning. Al-Sanusi argues that genuine incapacity is virtually non-existent and that obligation remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a critique, a citation of earlier scholars, a classification by Sharif, and a refutation by Sanusi.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the available argument.
2
Identify Sharif's two types of i'tiqad
Sharif divides belief into two categories: belief contrary to reality (ignorance) and belief conforming to reality. The latter is further subdivided into nazar-based and taqlid-based, with a distinction based on capability.
This classification is key to understanding the debate.
3
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi rejects the notion that some people are truly incapable of nazar. He asserts that anyone with basic intellect is capable, and difficulty does not remove obligation.
He even challenges the premise that obligating the impossible is impossible.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why this matters: if taqlid is acceptable, faith could be passive; Sanusi insists on active reasoning for true iman.
This connects to broader issues of responsibility and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عود لتقبيح التقليد ، ورد صحة القول بإيمان المقلد وقد حكى الأستاذ أبو إسحاق رضي الله تعالى عنه : أن المحققين قالوا : لا يتصف بصفة الإيمان إلا من له دليل على كل ركن من أركان الدين ؛ قال : ( وإنما ذهب إلى قبول التقليد أهل الظاهر ونقل ابن القصار وغيره عن مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه وجوب النظر ، وأن التقليد في العقائد لا يكفي وقال الشريف أبو يحيى في شرح الإرشاد : ( وعندي : أن الاعتقاد على ضربين : اعتقاد المعلوم على نقيض ما هو عليه ؛ وهو حقيقة الجهل والجاهل بالله تعالى كافر . واعتقاد المعلوم على ما هو عليه ؛ فإن كان نظراً فهو المقصود ، وإن كان تقليداً : فإما أن يكون المكلف ممن فيه فضل للنظر والاستدلال ، أو لا ؛ فإن كان الأول فهو مؤمن عاص ، وإن كان الثاني فهو مؤمن وليس بعاص، وإلا لزم تكليف ما لا يطاق ، وأنه محال ؛ إما عقلاً عند قوم ، وإما شرعاً عند آخرين، وأما ما منعوه من التقليد فإنما ذلك في حق المتمكن من النظر والاستدلال ، وإلا لزم تكليف المحال على ما قررناه ) انتهى قلت : وما أشار إليه من العجز عن النظر هو في غاية الندور ، أو هو ليس بموجود أصلاً ؛ فإن الظاهر أن كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف فهو متمكن من المعرفة والنظر، وقصارى الأمر : أن النظر الصحيح يعسر على قوم ويسهل على آخرين، والعسر ليس مما يسقط التكليف في كثير من الفروع ، فكيف بأصول الإيمان ؟! ثم على تقدير تسليم وجوده كما ادعاه الشريف ، وأن تكليفه بالنظر تكليف بما لا يطاق .. فلا نسلم أن التكليف بما لا يطاق غير واقع في
Return to condemning taqlid and refuting the validity of the statement that the iman of the muqallid [is valid]. And Abu Ishaq (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated that the verifiers said: No one is described with the attribute of iman except one who has evidence for every pillar of the religion. He said: Only the people of dhahir went to accepting taqlid. And Ibn al-Qassar and others transmitted from Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) the obligation of nazar, and that taqlid in beliefs is not sufficient. And al-Sharif Abu Yahya said in Sharh al-Irshad: In my view, i'tiqad is of two types: 1) Belief that the known thing is contrary to what it is; that is the reality of ignorance, and the one ignorant of Allah is a disbeliever. 2) Belief that the known thing is as it is; if it is based on nazar, that is the intended [belief]; and if it is based on taqlid, then either the responsible person has capacity for nazar and inference, or not. If the first, then he is a believer but disobedient; if the second, then he is a believer and not disobedient. Otherwise, it would entail the obligation of what is not possible, and that is impossible—either rationally according to some, or legally according to others. And as for their prohibition of taqlid, that is only regarding the one capable of nazar and inference; otherwise it would entail the obligation of the impossible, as we have established. End. I say: What he alluded to regarding inability for nazar is extremely rare, or it does not exist at all; for it is apparent that everyone who has the basic intellect of obligation is capable of knowledge and nazar. The upshot is that correct nazar is difficult for some and easy for others, and difficulty does not negate obligation in many subsidiary matters—so how about the principles of iman? Then, on the assumption of conceding its existence as Sharif claimed, and that obligating him to nazar is obligating what is impossible, we do not concede that obligating what is impossible does not occur in
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a critique of taqlid in matters of belief, specifically responding to a classification by al-Sharif Abu Yahya. It begins by recapitulating the condemnation of taqlid and rejecting the validity of considering a muqallid (blind follower) as a true believer. The author cites Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who reports that the verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) hold that a person is only described as having iman if he has evidence for every pillar of religion; those who accept taqlid are the people of dhahir (literalists). Then it quotes Ibn al-Qassar and others attributing to Imam Malik the obligation of nazar (theoretical reasoning) and that taqlid in doctrines (aqa'id) is insufficient. The author then presents the view of al-Sharif Abu Yahya from his commentary on al-Irshad. Sharif divides i'tiqad (belief) into two types: (1) belief contrary to reality, which is true ignorance, and the one ignorant of Allah is a disbeliever; (2) belief conforming to reality. The second type is further subdivided: if it is based on nazar, that is the intended proper belief; if it is based on taqlid, then the person may either be capable of nazar and inference or not. If capable but uses taqlid, he is a believer but sinful; if incapable, he is a believer and not sinful. Otherwise, obligating what is beyond one's capacity would be impossible. Sharif argues that the prohibition of taqlid applies only to those capable of nazar, to avoid obligating the impossible. After quoting Sharif, the author (al-Sanusi) responds with 'I say' (qultu). He rejects the idea that true inability for nazar exists, calling it extremely rare or non-existent. He asserts that everyone with basic intellect (asl 'aql al-taklif) is capable of knowledge and nazar. He concedes that correct nazar may be difficult for some, but difficulty does not remove obligation in subsidiary matters, much less in the fundamentals of iman. Even if one granted that such inability exists as Sharif claimed, and that obligating nazar would be obligating the impossible, al-Sanusi denies that obligating the impossible is impossible. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the argument on the next page. The overall thrust is a strong rejection of taqlid in matters of belief, insisting on the obligation of nazar for all who are intellectually capable.

Misconception: Some people are genuinely incapable of reasoning in matters of belief, so taqlid is excusable for them.
Correction: Al-Sanusi argues that genuine incapacity is extremely rare or non-existent; everyone with basic intellect is capable of nazar. Difficulty does not remove obligation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sanusi's response, what is the status of the claim that some people are incapable of nazar in matters of belief?
A) It is a common and valid excuse.
B) It is extremely rare or non-existent.
C) It applies only to women.
D) It is accepted without question.

Key Takeaway
True faith requires personal conviction through reasoning; the excuse of incapacity is virtually non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one core belief you hold about Allah or the unseen that you have never personally verified through reasoning or study. Is your faith in that matter built on taqlid? What concrete step can you take this week to examine it with evidence?
?
You often hear that 'difficulty does not negate obligation.' Identify one obstacle—whether laziness, busyness, or pride—that you have used to avoid deep study of aqeedah. How can you overcome that obstacle starting today?
?
If your belief in Allah is not based on personal nazar, are you willing to risk that it might be mere imitation? What would it take for you to move from inherited faith to a faith built on certainty, even if it requires effort? Commit to one specific action.

Refuting Al-Sharif and Al-Qarafi's Example of Taklif Ma La Yutaq
Sanusi refutes al-Sharif's claim of incapacity and cites al-Qarafi on the occurrence of taklif ma la yutaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that taklif ma la yutaq does not occur in usul al-din, using both logical argument and transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
Even if some are incapable, obligation of the impossible does occur in fundamentals.

Sanusi tackles a critical objection: that obligating the incapable is impossible and thus not valid in religious fundamentals. He first questions the reality of total incapacity, then proves that even if it exists, it does not negate obligation, citing al-Qarafi's example of a weak-minded woman in remote lands. This lesson examines both parts of his argument and the severe implications for those who fail to attain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Sanusi's confident tone as he dismisses the opponent's claim.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
Sanusi first argues that total incapacity to reason is extremely rare or nonexistent. Then he grants the possibility for argument's sake and refutes the claim that taklif ma la yutaq does not occur in usul al-din, using al-Qarafi's report.
Label these as Part 1 (refutation of existence) and Part 2 (refutation of non-occurrence).
3
Analyze al-Qarafi's example
Al-Qarafi gives the example of a weak-minded woman in extreme regions who cannot understand but is still obligated and punished. Understand why this is considered an instance of taklif ma la yutaq.
Note the Quranic verse cited about the Turks not understanding speech.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider the severity: even if ignorance is necessary and unrepellable, the person is still sinful and punished. This shows that in usul al-din, obligation can be impossible yet still binding.
This contradicts the opponent's claim and affirms Ash'ari doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : وما أشار إليهِ مِنَ العجز عن النظر هو في غاية الندور ، أو هو ليس بموجود أصلاً ؛ فإنَّ الظاهر أنَّ كلَّ مَنْ مَعَهُ أَصلُ عقل التكليف فهو مُتمكِّنْ مِنَ المعرفة والنظر (۲) ، وقُصارى الأمرِ : أنَّ النظر الصحيح يعسر على قوم ويسهل على آخرينَ ، والعسر ليسَ ممَّا يُسقِط التكليف في كثيرٍ مِنَ الفروع ، فكيف بأصول الإيمان ؟! ثم على تقدير تسليم وجودِهِ كما ادعاه الشريف ، وأنَّ تكليفه بالنظرِ تكليف بما لا يُطاقُ .. فلا نُسلّمُ أنَّ التكليف بما لا يُطاقُ غيرُ واقعِ في أصول الدين ، وما ادعاه الشريف مِنْ عدم وقوعِهِ فيها بمُجرَّدِ اجتهاده.. مُعارَضُ بنقل القرافي ضدَّ ذلك ، قال القرافي - على ما رأيتُ في نقل بعض المشايخ عنه - : ( وقد شدَّدَ صاحب الشرع في عقائدِ أصول الدين تشديداً عظيماً ؛ بحيثُ إِنَّ الإنسان لو بذل جهدَهُ واستفرغ وُسْعَهُ في رفع الجهلِ عنه في صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِ اللهِ تعالى أو في شيء مما يجبُ اعتقادُهُ مِنْ أصول الدين ، ولم يرتفع ذلك الجهلُ عنه . . فإنَّهُ آثم كافرُ على المشهورِ مِنَ المذاهب ، معَ أَنَّهُ قد أوصل الاجتهاد حدَّهُ ، وصارَ الجهل له ضرورياً لا يمكنه دفعُهُ ، ومع ذلك لم يُعذَر ، حتى صارَتْ هذه الصورة ممَّا يُعتقَدُ أَنَّهَا مِنْ وقوع تكليف ما لا يُطاق ؛ فإنَّ تكليف المرأةِ البلهاء الفاسدة المزاج في الأقاليم المنحرفة عمَّا يُوجِبُ استقامة العقل ؛ كأقاصي بلادِ السُّودان وأقاصي بلادِ التركِ .. ممَّا لا يُطاقُ ؛ فإنَّ هذهِ الأقاليم لا يكونُ للعقل فيها كبير رونق ، ولذلك قال تعالى في بلادِ التركِ وَجَدَ مِن دُونِهِمَا قَوْمًا لَّا يَكَادُونَ يَفْقَهُونَ قَوْلًا ﴾ [الكهف : ۹۳] ، ومع ذلك فهم مُكلَّفونَ بدقائقِ أصولِ الدينِ ودلائل التوحيد ، ومُخلَّدونَ بالجهلِ في النارِ ((۱)
I say: What he indicated about inability to engage in reasoning is extremely rare, or rather it does not exist at all; for it is apparent that everyone who possesses the basic intellect of moral obligation is capable of knowledge and reasoning (2). The most that can be said is that sound reasoning is difficult for some people and easy for others, and difficulty does not remove the obligation in many subsidiary matters, so how [can it] in the fundamentals of faith?! Then, even if we grant its existence as al-Sharif claimed, and that obligating him to reason is an obligation of what is not possible... we do not concede that the obligation of what is not possible does not occur in the fundamentals of religion. What al-Sharif claimed—that it does not occur there based on his mere ijtihad—is contradicted by the transmission from al-Qarafi to the contrary. Al-Qarafi said—according to what I have seen in a transmission from some shaykhs about him—: 'The Lawgiver has placed an extremely severe emphasis on the creeds of the fundamentals of religion, such that if a person exerts his effort and expends all his capacity to remove ignorance from himself concerning an attribute of Allah the Exalted or something that must be believed from the fundamentals of religion, but that ignorance is not removed from him... then he is a sinful disbeliever according to the well-known [view] among the schools, even though he has brought ijtihad to its fullest, and ignorance has become necessary for him and he cannot repel it, and yet he is not excused. Thus this scenario has come to be considered an instance of the occurrence of the obligation of what is not possible; for the obligation imposed on a weak-minded woman of corrupt temperament in regions deviating from what necessitates soundness of intellect—like the farthest lands of the Sudan and the farthest lands of the Turks—is impossible; for these regions do not have much brilliance of intellect. Therefore Allah the Exalted said concerning the land of the Turks: "He found beyond them a people who could scarcely understand speech" [al-Kahf 93]. Yet they are obligated with the intricacies of the fundamentals of religion and the proofs of tawhid, and they are eternally [punished] in the Fire for their ignorance.'
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is Sanusi's response to an opponent (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani) who argued that some people are incapable of rational inquiry (nazar) and that obligating them is impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). Sanusi first contests the existence of such total incapacity, saying it is extremely rare or nonexistent; everyone with basic intellect is capable. Difficulty does not remove obligation. Then, even if he grants the existence of such incapacity for argument's sake, he refutes the claim that taklif ma la yutaq does not occur in usul al-din. He cites al-Qarafi, who reports that the Lawgiver has been extremely severe: if a person exerts full effort but remains ignorant about an attribute of Allah, he is deemed a sinful disbeliever, even though ignorance became necessary for him. Al-Qarafi gives the example of a weak-minded woman in extreme regions (like the farthest Sudan and Turks) where intellect is dull, yet they are obligated with the intricacies of religion and proofs of tawhid, and are punished eternally for ignorance. This, al-Qarafi says, is an instance of taklif ma la yutaq actually occurring. Thus Sanusi proves that the obligation of what is not possible does occur in the fundamentals of religion, contrary to al-Sharif's claim.

Key Terms

نظر

أصول الدين
قرافي
اجتهاد
بلهاء
أقاصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a person cannot understand a religious truth due to inherent incapacity, they are excused from belief and punishment.
Correction: Al-Qarafi's example shows that even a weak-minded woman in remote regions who cannot understand is obligated with the intricacies of faith and is punished for ignorance, making it an instance of taklif ma la yutaq.

Checkpoint

A) Excused
B) A sinful disbeliever
C) A believer without sin
D) Not obligated

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that taklif ma la yutaq does occur in usul al-din through transmitted evidence from al-Qarafi, refuting the opponent's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
