النطق بالأدلَّةِ والتفاصحُ بها ورد الشبه قد لا يلازم المعرفة ، وقد يحصل العكس . وقد ينطقُ بالأدلَّةِ المتقنة ، ويتفاصحُ بها ، ويردُّ الشبهات .. مَنْ لا معرفة عندَه ، بل قد يكون مرتاباً ، ويَعجز عن ذلكَ مَنْ هو عارفٌ ، وقد كانَتِ العامَّةُ في الأزمنة الماضية لأدناهم وسُوقتِهم مِنْ تحقيق العلم النافع في أصل الدين وفروعه وجودةِ الفهم لذلك. لذلك .. ما لا يُوجَدُ مثلُه واللهِ في الماهرِ منْ أكابر علمائنا . وقد أدرك ابنُ العربي وغيرُهُ مِنَ المتأخرين بقايا تلك الأزمنة المشرقة ، وحَكَوا عن عامَّتِها وصغار صبيانها حكايات ظريفة في تحقيقهم للعلوم ، ومعرفتهم بطُرُقِ فَهْمِها ، بحيثُ يُستغرَبُ أَنْ يقعَ
Speaking with proofs, and eloquent articulation with them, and refuting doubts does not necessarily accompany knowledge, and the opposite may occur. For someone may speak with precise proofs, and articulate eloquently with them, and refute doubts, while he has no knowledge, rather he may be in doubt; and someone who is knowledgeable may be incapable of that. And the common people in past times—even the lowest of them and their market-goers—had from verification of beneficial knowledge in the fundamentals of religion and its branches and keenness of understanding for it, something not found—by Allah—in the skilled among our greatest scholars. And Ibn ʿArabī and others of the later scholars witnessed remnants of those shining times, and related from their common people and young boys remarkable stories in their verification of the sciences and their knowledge of ways of understanding them, such that one would be amazed that it occurs.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration that articulate expression of proofs and refutation of doubts does not necessarily indicate true knowledge. The author states that the opposite can happen: a person might present precise proofs and refute doubts while being ignorant or even doubtful, while a true knower may be unable to do so. He then gives a historical example: the common people in past times—even the lowest and the market-goers—had such deep verification of beneficial knowledge in the fundamentals and branches of religion, and such keen understanding, that the best of our skilled scholars cannot match it. He mentions that Ibn ʿArabī and other later scholars encountered remnants of those shining times and related remarkable stories of common people and young boys demonstrating profound understanding of the sciences and their ways, to an astonishing degree.

Misconception: One might think that eloquent speech about proofs is a reliable sign of true knowledge and faith.
Correction: The author warns that such eloquence may be present in someone without knowledge, even in doubt, while a true knower may lack such articulation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about eloquence in presenting proofs?
A) It always indicates true knowledge.
B) It is never found in a true believer.
C) It may be present in someone without knowledge.
D) It is only found in scholars.

Key Takeaway
Eloquence in proving faith is not a reliable indicator of genuine belief; deep understanding can be found in unexpected places.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Reality of Knowledge and the Status of Laypeople
Sanusi argues true knowledge is in the heart, not verbal articulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true knowledge (ma'rifa) resides in the heart and is not necessarily linked to the ability to articulate proofs, and that we should not judge laypeople's faith based on their verbal expression.

Core Idea
True knowledge is in the heart, not on the tongue.

Sanusi addresses a common misconception: that someone who cannot articulate theological proofs must be ignorant or a blind follower. He argues that understanding in the heart is what matters, and that many scholars themselves struggle to express what they know. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications for how we view laypeople and past generations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice that Sanusi uses a dense but clear style.
2
Identify the key condition for knowledge
Find where Sanusi states that the ability to articulate is not a condition; rather, understanding in the heart is required.
This is the central thesis.
3
Note the exception and the warning
Sanusi allows an exception: if a person's speech reveals a corrupt belief, then we must gently teach. Also, he warns that verbal fluency can be divorced from true knowledge.
4
Reflect on the examples from past generations
Consider how Sanusi uses the common people of earlier times and the righteous predecessors (salaf) to show that deep understanding is possible even among those who cannot articulate well.
He makes a powerful a fortiori argument.
5
Consider the present situation and conclusion
Sanusi laments the neglect of beneficial knowledge in his time and declares that anyone who attains it today experiences a miracle. End with the prayer.
This reinforces the rarity and value of genuine knowledge.

النطق بالأدلَّةِ والتفاصحُ بها وبرد الشبه قد لا يلازم المعرفة ، وقد يحصل العكس.

وإذا كانَتْ عامَّةُ هذهِ الأزمنة مع تأخُرِها وبُعْدِها بكثيرٍ عن زمانِ النبوَّةِ وفيضان أنوارها .. حصلَ لهم مِنْ تحقيق العلم ما حصل ، فكيفَ يكونُ في تحقيق العلم النافع ومعرفة السنة عامة السلف الصالح ونساؤهم وصبياتهم وعبيدهم وإماؤُهم ؟! كيف وهم الممتثلونَ قولَ اللهِ عزَّ وجل : ( يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا قُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ وَأَهْلِيكُمْ نَارًا) [التحريم : ٦] ؟! وأينَ تلك الأزمنة وما يقربُ مِنَ القريب منها مِنْ هذه الأزمنة المظلمة جدّاً التي أدركناها ونشأنا نحنُ فيها ؟! والله المستعان ، ولا حول لنا ولا قوَّةَ إلا بالله ، فإنَّ العلم النافع اليومَ أُهمِلَ تَعلُّمُهُ إهمالاً عظيماً ، وتُرِكَ تعليمُهُ للأهل والولد ، فكيف للعبيد والإماء عند الناس كالحيوان البهيمي الذي سقط عنه التكليف الذين هم ولا يُقصَدُ إلا لقضاء المآرب الدنيوية ؟! حتى إِنَّكَ لا تجد اليوم تحقيق علم نافع ولا سماعَهُ على وجهِهِ مِنْ أكثرَ ممَّنْ يتعاطاه ؛ لقلَّةِ أهل العلم في أزمنتِنا جداً ، فمَنْ رُزِقَ اليوم تحقيق ما يخصُّهُ في دينِهِ ، ووُفِّقَ للعمل به .. فلا شك أَنَّهُ قد خُرِقَتْ لهُ العادة في هذا الزمن الكثيرِ الفسادِ .
نسألُ الله سبحانَهُ أنْ يعاملنا إلى المماتِ بفضلِهِ ، ولا يحيد بنا عن
And if you know that the ability to articulate the proof and express it is not a condition for knowledge (ma'rifa) and leaving taqlid, but rather understanding it in the heart is what is required... then we should not think ill of a layman (ammi) or others, nor should we definitively judge him to be a taqlid merely because his tongue is incapable of articulating the proofs of the beliefs (aqaid), due to the possibility that he may be knowledgeable in the beliefs of faith and their proofs, but in a way that makes it difficult for him to express them. Many scholars are unable to express what is in their consciences of verified sciences, so how about the common people? Unless there appears on a person's tongue what indicates the corrupt belief that is hidden in his conscience; then it is obligatory to be gentle in teaching him and curing his ailment as much as possible. And God is the Succour. Verbal utterance of proofs, fluency in them, and refutation of specious arguments (shubuhat) may not accompany knowledge, and the opposite may occur. One may articulate precise proofs, be fluent in them, and refute specious arguments, but have no knowledge; indeed he may be in doubt. And one who is knowledgeable may be incapable of that. The common people in past times, even the lowest of them and their market-goers, had such realization of beneficial knowledge in the fundamentals of religion and its branches, and such quality of understanding, that the like of it is not found, by God, even in the skilled among our greatest scholars. Ibn al-Arabi and others of the later scholars witnessed remnants of those bright times, and related from their common people and young children delightful stories of their realization of sciences and their knowledge of the ways of understanding them, such that one would be amazed if a little of that occurred from our greatest scholars, let alone from our common people! This, while they grew up in later times in which corruption increased relative to before, and their scholars frequently condemned them and regarded their state as grave. And if the common people of these times, despite their lateness and great distance from the time of prophethood and the outpouring of its lights, attained such realization of knowledge, what then about the realization of beneficial knowledge and knowledge of the Sunnah among the general righteous predecessors (salaf salih), their women, their young girls, their male and female slaves?! For they were those who complied with the saying of God Almighty: 'O you who have believed, protect yourselves and your families from a Fire' (At-Tahrim: 6). Where are those times and what is near to them compared to these extremely dark times in which we have lived and grown up?! God is the Succour. There is no power and no strength except in God. Beneficial knowledge today has been greatly neglected in learning it, and its teaching to family and children has been abandoned, so how about (teaching) slaves and maidservants, who are treated like dumb animals upon whom religious obligation is dropped, and who are not intended except for fulfilling worldly needs?! So much so that you do not find today a realization of beneficial knowledge nor hearing it in its proper manner from most of those who practice it, due to the extreme scarcity of people of knowledge in our times. So whoever is granted today the realization of what pertains to him in his religion and is guided to act upon it, there is no doubt that a miracle (khariq lidha al-ada) has occurred for him in this age of great corruption. We ask God, glorified be He, to treat us with His grace until death, and not to turn us away from
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of true knowledge (ma'rifa) and its relationship to verbal expression. It begins by establishing that the ability to articulate a proof and express it is not a condition for possessing knowledge or for leaving blind following (taqlid). Instead, the essential condition is understanding in the heart. From this, Sanusi draws a practical conclusion: we must not assume ignorance of a layman (ammi) or anyone else based solely on their inability to verbalize proofs, because they may actually know the beliefs and their proofs in a way that is difficult to express. He supports this by noting that many scholars are unable to articulate what they truly know, so laymen are even more likely to be in that situation. However, an exception is made if a person's speech reveals a corrupt belief (aqd fasid) in their heart; then it becomes obligatory to gently teach and cure them.

Then Sanusi makes a broader observation: fluency in articulating proofs and refuting specious arguments (shubuhat) does not necessarily accompany true knowledge. In fact, the opposite can happen: someone may speak eloquently and refute doubts but lack genuine knowledge—even being in doubt themselves—while another who truly knows may be unable to express it. He then reflects on past generations, stating that the common people of earlier times (even the lowest and the market-goers) possessed such a high level of realization of beneficial knowledge and understanding in religion that it surpassed even the most skilled among later scholars. He cites Ibn al-Arabi and other later scholars who witnessed the remnants of those bright times and related remarkable stories of the deep understanding of common people and even young children, which would be astonishing if it came from later great scholars, let alone their commoners. He emphasizes that those earlier generations grew up in later times that were already corrupt relative to what came before, yet they still attained such knowledge.

He then uses an a fortiori argument: if the common people of his own (later) times, distant from prophethood, still achieved realization of knowledge, how much greater must have been the knowledge of the righteous predecessors (salaf salih), including their women, children, and slaves, who lived closer to the prophetic era and followed God's command to protect themselves and their families from the Fire (Quran 66:6). He contrasts these bright times with the extremely dark times he himself experienced, where beneficial knowledge is largely neglected, not taught to families, and slaves are treated like animals without religious obligation. He laments that today one cannot find true beneficial knowledge or proper transmission of it among most practitioners due to the scarcity of scholars. Finally, he declares that anyone who today attains true knowledge and acts upon it has experienced a miracle (khariq lil-ada) in this corrupt age, and he concludes with a prayer for God's grace.

Correction: Sanusi explains that true knowledge resides in the heart and may not be expressible; many scholars themselves struggle to articulate what they know. Therefore, we should not judge based on verbal ability alone.

The Mu'tazila on Human Creation of Actions and Takfir
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila claim and the ruling on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that humans invent their own actions and the Sunni ruling that they are considered disbelievers.

Core Idea
Claiming humans create their own actions contradicts tawhid and leads to disbelief.

Sanusi now presents the third and most controversial type of attributing action to other than Allah: the Mu'tazila doctrine that humans invent their own actions. This doctrine touches on divine justice and human responsibility. Sanusi then explains the Sunni position on whether such a view takes one out of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the Mu'tazila claim and their reasoning.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila argument
Notice their claim that if Allah commanded what the servant does not create, it would be injustice. Why does this reasoning lead to heresy?
3
Understand the ruling on takfir
Note the disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna and the more apparent view that they are disbelievers. Consider the evidence from al-Qadi Abu Bakr and the hadith.
The hadith curses the Qadariyya, indicating the gravity of this belief.
4
Reflect on the hadith
The hadith states Qadariyya were cursed on the tongue of seventy prophets. What does this say about the danger of this belief?

النوع الثالث مما أضيف الفعل فيه إلى غير الله سبحانه: ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة من أن العبد يخترع أفعال نفسه، وقالوا: لو كلف الله تعالى بما لا يخترعه العبد.. لكان جورا وظلما! وقد اختلف أهل السنة في تكفيرهم، والأظهر أنهم كافرون وهذا مذهب القاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب في تكفير من يؤول به قوله إلى الكفر ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لعنت القدرية على لسان سبعين»

And Ahl al-Sunna disagreed about their takfir, and the more apparent is that they are disbelievers. And this is the madhhab of al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib in takfir of one whose statement leads him to disbelief, and because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'The Qadariyya were cursed on the tongue of seventy...'
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage presents the third type of attributing action to other than Allah: the Mu'tazilite claim that the human servant invents (creates) his own actions. Their argument: if Allah commanded what the servant does not create, that would be unjust. The author notes that Ahl al-Sunna disagree on whether to declare them disbelievers, but the more apparent opinion is that they are disbelievers. He attributes this view to al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib, and supports it with the hadith: 'The Qadariyya were cursed on the tongue of seventy prophets' (or similar) – the hadith is cut off.

The Salaf's Consensus on Seeing Allah
Sanusi presents consensus of Salaf and necessity of ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the belief in seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife is established by the consensus of the Salaf and is known from the religion by necessity.

Core Idea
Ru'ya is known by consensus and necessity, not just reason.

In this passage, Imam Sanusi presents two powerful arguments for the reality of seeing Allah in the Hereafter. First, he points to the unanimous desire of the righteous predecessors (Salaf) to behold their Lord. Second, he asserts that this vision is so clearly taught in Islam that it is almost known by necessity. This lesson unpacks these proofs and their implications for faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's words speak first.
Notice the concise style and the shift from argument to prayer.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find where Sanusi presents the consensus (ijma') of the Salaf and where he states that ru'ya is known from religion by necessity.
The second argument is phrased as 'yakad an yakuna' (almost such that).
3
Reflect on the prayer
Consider why the author ends with a supplication asking for the vision of Allah. How does this reinforce the preceding arguments?
The prayer shows personal longing and reliance on divine grace.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the main claim of the passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإجماع : فلا خفاء أن السلف الصالح معلوم من حالهم الرغبة إلى الله تعالى أن يمتعهم بالنظر إليه جل وعز . وبالجملة : فثبوت الرؤية يكاد أن يكون مما علم من الدين ضرورة . نسأله سبحانه ألا يحرمنا من النظر إليه نظر أهل الخصوص من أوليائه، وأهل معرفته، وأكرم أهل الجنة عليه، بجاه سيدنا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وشرَّف وكرَّم، ثم بجاه ملائكته وجميع رسله وأنبيائه .
As for consensus: it is no secret that the righteous Salaf—it is known from their state—[had] desire for Allah Most High to grant them enjoyment of looking at Him, Mighty and Majestic. And in summary: the affirmation of vision [of Allah] is almost such that it is known from the religion by necessity. We ask Him, exalted is He, not to deprive us of looking at Him—the look of the special ones among His saints, and those who know Him, and the most honored of the people of Paradise before Him—by the rank of our master and patron Muhammad (may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and honor and venerate him), then by the rank of His angels and all His messengers and prophets.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife. First, it cites the consensus (ijma') of the Salaf, the early righteous Muslims, whose desire to see Allah indicates their belief in its possibility. The author states that it is well-known from their state that they yearned for this vision. Second, it claims that the affirmation of ru'ya is so firmly established that it is almost known from the religion by necessity (daruratan)—that is, it is a self-evident truth of Islam. The passage concludes with a prayer asking Allah to grant the reader and believers the honor of that vision in Paradise, invoking the rank of the Prophet Muhammad and other prophets and angels.

Misconception: Some think ru'ya is only known from rational argument, not from religious sources.
Correction: Sanusi emphasizes that ru'ya is known from the consensus of the Salaf and is almost a necessity of the religion, showing its strong scriptural and communal foundation.

Checkpoint
How does the author describe the status of ru'ya in this passage?

D) It is not affirmed by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Ru'ya of Allah is affirmed by the consensus of the Salaf and is a necessary belief in Islam.

The Obligation of Believing All That the Prophet Brought
Sanusi concludes that faith in all unseen matters reported by Sharia is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that faith in all unseen matters reported by the Sharia is obligatory, and you will be familiar with the specific eschatological beliefs that every mukallaf must accept.

Core Idea
Believing everything the Prophet brought from Allah is obligatory—not optional.

In this concluding passage, Sanusi summarizes the key sam'iyyat (audible matters) that every Muslim must believe. He emphasizes that the Prophet is to be trusted in all he conveyed from Allah, and then lists the major eschatological events. He also clarifies that the purpose of his summary is to help the student move beyond blind imitation (taqlid) in matters of creed.

Guided Path

2

Each item is introduced by 'كـ' (like) indicating examples.
3
Understand the consensus mentions
Sanusi explicitly says two of these are by consensus (إجماعاً): resurrection of this very body and intercession for sinful believers after some have entered the Fire.
This shows the importance of scholarly agreement in creed.
4
Grasp the book's purpose
Sanusi states that the intention of this summary is to help the mukallaf leave taqlid. Reflect on why learning these fundamentals frees one from blind imitation.
He says understanding these sentences suffices for that purpose.

Faith in the unseen matters that the Law has informed about. So it is obligatory to believe him (peace be upon him) in everything he brought from Allah Almighty: like the resurrection of this very body, not its likeness, by consensus, and similar things like the questioning of the grave, its bliss and its punishment, and the Bridge, and the Scale, and the Basin, and intercession for the disobedient believers to save them from the Fire after the threat has been carried out on a group of them, by consensus, and the eternal bliss of the believers and punishment of the disbelievers. Then it clarifies that the details of what the Prophet brought are in fiqh and hadith books, but the purpose of this summary is to enable the mukallaf to leave taqlid (blind imitation) in beliefs. Understanding these sentences suffices for that purpose. The passage ends with prayers and praise.

Bid'ah Nullifies Deeds: Evidence from Quranic Indication
The author equates bid'ah with shirk in nullifying deeds and proves it via Quranic implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how bid'ah nullifies good deeds, why it is equated with shirk, and the Quranic evidence from daleel al-khitab.

Core Idea
Bid'ah cancels good deeds just as shirk does—based on Quranic implication.

In this lesson, we explore a powerful claim: that religious innovation (bid'ah) can nullify all good deeds, just like shirk. The author supports this with a subtle but strong Quranic argument using the principle of daleel al-khitab. Let's see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author compares bid'ah to shirk in nullifying deeds.
The author does not explain why bid'ah is like shirk; he simply asserts it.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
The verse from Surah Al-Kahf (18:30) is cited. Understand how daleel al-khitab is used to derive the opposite ruling.
Daleel al-khitab is the implication that if a condition is stated, its opposite implies the contrary.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if following the sunna ensures reward, what happens to those who oppose it? The passage says their deeds are not accepted.
This is a strong warning against innovation.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider why bid'ah is considered so dangerous—it can negate even righteous deeds.
This underscores the importance of adhering to prophetic teachings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البدعة تُحبِط العمل كما يُحبِطُهُ الشرك والشرك كما يُحبط الأعمال كذلك البدعة تُحبط الأعمال ولذلك قيل في قوله تعالى : {إنا لا نضيع أجر من أحسن عملا} [الكهف : 30] هو من اتبع السنة ففيه دليل من دليل الخطاب أن من خالف السنة لم يقبل منه عمل وإن كان صالحا (انتهى)
Bid'ah nullifies deeds just as shirk nullifies them, and shirk nullifies deeds; likewise bid'ah nullifies deeds. Therefore it was said concerning His statement, Most High: {Indeed, We do not waste the reward of those who do good} [Al-Kahf: 30]: He is the one who follows the sunna. So in this is an indication from the indication of the text (daleel al-khitab) that whoever opposes the sunna, no deed is accepted from him even if it is righteous. (End)
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This is a classification and proof passage. The author makes a parallel between bid'ah (innovation) and shirk (polytheism), stating that both nullify good deeds. He then supports this with a Quranic argument: the verse 'إنا لا نضيع أجر من أحسن عملا' (We do not waste the reward of those who do good) is explained to mean the one who follows the sunna. Using the principle of 'daleel al-khitab' (implication of the text), the author deduces that the opposite case—those who oppose the sunna—will have their deeds rejected, even if those deeds are apparently righteous. Thus, the passage establishes a strong warning against innovation by equating it with shirk in its effect on deeds.

شرك
دليل الخطاب
سنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only shirk nullifies good deeds; bid'ah is less severe.
Correction: The passage teaches that bid'ah also nullifies deeds, and it provides scriptural evidence that whoever opposes the sunna (i.e., commits bid'ah) has no deed accepted.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Bid'ah nullifies good deeds in a manner similar to shirk, as derived from the Quranic principle of daleel al-khitab.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the weight of this: even righteous deeds, if built on innovation, are rejected. Has your love for a particular ritual ever blinded you to its lack of scriptural basis? Will you endure the discomfort of leaving it for Allah's pleasure?

The Sunni Middle Position on Sinful Believers
Sanusi defines the Sunni position on the fasiq between Mu'tazila/Khawarij and Murji'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the fate of unrepentant sinners and how it avoids the extremes of the Mu'tazila/Khawarij and Murji'a.

Core Idea
The Sunni position is the balanced middle path between excessive punishment and excessive mercy.

Sanusi presents the Sunni consensus that all Muslims will ultimately enter Paradise, but some may be punished temporarily for their sins. This position is carefully distinguished from two extreme views: the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who claim eternal punishment for unrepentant sinners, and the Murji'a, who claim unconditional pardon. As we read, note how Sanusi grounds this position in revelation and then draws an analogy to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb) to show the consistent middle path of Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Let the author's own presentation of the Sunni position sink in without analyzing yet.
Notice the structure: first the position, then refutations, then the middle, then the analogy.
2
Identify the two extreme views
What do the Mu'tazila/Khawarij say about the fate of major sinners? What do the Murji'a say? Notice how the Sunni view is a middle ground.
The extremes are both considered 'corrupt doctrines' (madhhabayn fasidayn).
3
Reflect on the analogy to kasb
How does the Ash'ari middle position on human action mirror the middle position on punishment? What does this tell you about the method of Ahl al-Sunna?
The analogy shows a consistent pattern of avoiding extremes in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموتِ بإجماع أهل السنَّةِ ، ودخولهم الجنةَ
- إما ابتداءً بغيرِ عقوبةٍ أصلاً ، لعفو الله تعالى أو شفاعةِ الشافعينَ
وإما بعد التعذيب بالنار على قَدْرِ الذنوبِ
لكن نقطعُ بنفوذ الوعيدِ في جماعة منهم مِنْ غيرِ تعيين ؛ لمجيء النصوص بذلكَ ، فبطل قولُ المعتزلة والخوارج القائلين بخلودِ ذوي الكبائرِ غيرِ التائبين في النارِ ، وبطل مذهب المرجئة القائلين بتحتم العفو
عن كل عاص مؤمن ، وأنَّهُ لا يُعذَّبُ ولا يدخلُ النارَ إِلا الكفَّارُ فقط
•
فقولُ أهل السنَّةِ بأنَّ الفسقة غير التائبين في مشيئة الله تعالى ؛ يُعذِّبُ مَنْ يشاءُ ، يرحم مَنْ يشاءُ ، وأنَّهُ لا بدَّ لكلِّ مؤمنِ مِنَ النعيمِ المؤيَّدِ وإِنْ عُذِّبَ أَوَّلاً على ذنوبِهِ .. وسط بين المذهبين الفاسدين ؛ لم يُفْرِطوا كما قالَتِ المعتزلة والخوارج ، ولا فَرَّطوا كما قالَتِ المرجئة . وهذا نظير قولهم بالاكتساب للأفعالِ بالقدرة الحادثة مِنْ غيرِ أنْ تُؤثر فيها ، فتوسطوا في ذلك أيضاً بين القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأُمَّةِ القائلين بأنَّ القدرة الحادثة هي المؤثرةُ في وجودِ الأفعال على حسَبِ ما يشاء العبد ، وبين الجبريَّةِ القائلين بأنْ لا قدرة للعبد ولا اكتساب لهُ مطلقاً
Death [of Muslims] by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, and their entering Paradise - either initially without any punishment at all, due to the pardon of Allah or the intercession of intercessors, or after punishment in fire according to the measure of sins. But we are certain of the efficacy of the threat upon a group of them without specification, because the texts have come with that. Thus the statement of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij who say that the perpetrators of major sins who do not repent are eternal in the fire is invalidated, and the doctrine of the Murji'a who say that pardon is certain for every disobedient believer, and that he will not be punished and will not enter the fire except the disbelievers only, is invalidated. And the statement of Ahl al-Sunna that the immoral believers who do not repent are under the will of Allah: He punishes whom He wills, He grants mercy to whom He wills, and that every believer must have everlasting bliss even if he is punished first for his sins – is a middle between the two corrupt doctrines. They did not exceed as the Mu'tazila and Khawarij said, nor did they fall short as the Murji'a said. And this is analogous to their statement of acquisition for actions by the originated power without it being effective in them, so they adopted a middle position in that as well between the Qadariyya, the Magians of this nation, who say that the originated power is the effective one in the existence of actions according to what the servant wills, and the Jabriyya who say that there is no power for the servant and no acquisition for him at all.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and argument. It opens by stating the Sunni consensus that all Muslims who die will enter Paradise, either immediately or after punishment in accordance with their sins. It then asserts that the threat of punishment applies to an unspecified group of them, based on revealed texts. This refutes both the Mu'tazila/Khawarij (who claim eternal punishment for unrepentant sinners) and the Murji'a (who claim unconditional pardon). The Sunni position is presented as the middle path: the fate of the immoral believer (fasiq) is in God's will, and ultimately every believer will experience everlasting bliss, even if punished first. The passage concludes with an analogy to the doctrine of acquisition (kasb): the Ash'ariyya hold a middle position between the Qadariyya (who affirm full human power) and the Jabriyya (who deny all human power), affirming that the created power has no real effect but the agent acquires the action.

كبائر
اكتساب
قدرة حادثة
جبرية
قدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage says 'because the texts have come with that' (لمجيء النصوص), showing it is based on revelation.

Checkpoint

C) Their fate is in God's will; they may be punished temporarily but will ultimately enter Paradise.
D) They will be punished exactly for their sins and then stay in Hell forever.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on sinful believers is a divinely-revealed middle path between two corrupt extremes, and it is consistent with the middle path on human action (kasb).

Today, write a sincere dua asking Allah to place you among those who balance hope and fear.

The Role of Regret and Resolution in Repentance
Sanusi defines regret and resolution in tawba, then answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relationship between regret (nadam) and resolution ('azm) in the definition of repentance (tawba), and how scholars answer the objection that resolution may be impossible.

Core Idea
Genuine regret over sin necessarily includes a resolve not to repeat it.

Sanusi defines repentance (tawba) by first clarifying its essential component: regret (nadam). He then addresses a common objection about the necessity of resolution ('azm) to avoid future sin, showing that true regret already implies such a resolve when possible. This passage clarifies a subtle point in the theology of repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the definition of regret
The passage opens by defining regret (nadam) as sadness and pain over a past sin, with a wish it had not happened. This is the core of repentance.
Regret is not just emotion but includes a wish for the past to be different.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Some raised an objection: if future sin may be impossible (due to death, madness, etc.), how can resolution be required? The answer: resolution is conditional on the sin being remembered and within one's power.
Imam al-Haramayn's words support this conditional understanding.
4
Grasp the final verification
Sanusi concludes that mentioning resolution is not an extra condition but an explanation of what is already contained in genuine regret. True regret never lacks that resolve when ability and awareness are present.
This prevents making repentance too demanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى الندم: تحزن وتوجع على أن فعل، وتمني كونه لم يفعل. وقد يزاد في التوبة قيد آخر؛ وهو العزم على ترك المعاودة في المستقبل، واعترض: بأن فعل المعصية في المستقبل قد لا يخطر بالبال؛ لذهول أو جنون أو موت أو نحو ذلك، وقد لا يقتدر عليه لعروض آفة؛ كخرس في القذف، أو شلل أو جب في الزنا، فلا يتصور العزم على الترك؛ لما فيه من الإشعار بالقدرة والاختيار. وأجيب: بأن المراد العزم على الترك على تقدير الخطوة والاقتدار، حتى لو سلب القدرة لم يشترط العزم على الترك، وبهذا يشعر كلام إمام الحرمين. ثم التحقيق: أن ذكر العزم إنما هو للبيان والتقرير، لا للتقييد والاحتراز؛ إذ الندم على المعصية لقبحها لا يخلو عن ذلك العزم على تقدير الخطوة والاقتدار ألبتة.
And the meaning of regret (nadam): being sad and pained over something done, and wishing it had not been done. And another condition may be added in repentance (tawba): that is, the resolution to abandon returning (to the sin) in the future. And an objection was raised: that the act of disobedience in the future may not occur to the mind due to distraction, insanity, death, or similar; and one may not be capable of it due to an affliction arising, like muteness in the case of slander, or paralysis or castration in the case of fornication, so resolution to abandon it cannot be conceived because it implies ability and choice. And it was answered: that what is meant is the resolution to abandon, on the condition of it occurring to the mind and being able, so that if ability is removed, resolution to abandon is not required. And the words of Imam al-Haramayn indicate this. Then the verification (tahqeeq) is that the mention of resolution is only for clarification and explanation, not for restriction and exclusion; because regret over disobedience due to its ugliness never lacks that resolution, given the condition of it occurring to the mind and being able.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage defines the components of repentance (tawba). The author first defines regret (nadam) as a feeling of sadness and pain over a past sin, accompanied by the wish it had not been done. He then notes that some scholars add an extra condition: the resolution ('azm) to not commit the sin again. An objection is raised: since future disobedience may be impossible (due to death, madness, or physical incapacity), a resolution to avoid it cannot be genuinely formed because resolution implies ability and choice. The answer clarifies that the resolution is conditional on the sin occurring to the mind and being within one's power; if ability is removed, the resolution is not required. The author cites Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) for support. Finally, the author gives his own verification: mentioning resolution is not an additional independent condition but merely an explanation of what is already contained in genuine regret—true regret over the ugliness of sin necessarily implies a resolve not to repeat it, given the opportunity and ability.

Key Terms
توبة (tawba)
ندم (nadam)
عزم ('azm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think resolution ('azm) is an impossible extra condition that makes repentance unreachable.
Correction: The passage shows that resolution is only required when the sin is remembered and within one's power; genuine regret already implies it conditionally.

Checkpoint
What is the precise answer to the objection that resolution to abandon a future sin may be impossible due to death or insanity?
A) Resolution is never required.
B) Resolution is required only when the sin is possible and occurs to the mind.
C) Resolution is required absolutely regardless of circumstances.
D) Resolution is replaced by regret.

Key Takeaway
True repentance (tawba) consists of regret (nadam) for the ugliness of sin, which necessarily implies a resolve ('azm) to not repeat it when able.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you have regretted in the past. Did your regret include a firm resolution to never repeat it, or was it merely feeling bad? If the resolve was weak or absent, what does that say about the sincerity of your repentance according to this lesson? Now, what will you do about it?
?
Have you ever postponed repentance because you thought you would inevitably sin again? How does Sanusi's argument that genuine regret necessarily contains resolution confront that excuse? What would it look like for you to repent with full resolve starting today?
?
Consider a sin you committed that you are now physically or circumstantially prevented from repeating (e.g., slander after becoming mute, or other permanent barrier). According to this passage, resolution is not required in such a case, but genuine regret still is. Have you truly felt that regret? If not, what is holding you back?

True Repentance: Beyond Mere Words
Sanusi distinguishes common usage from true repentance and gives its sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between superficial repentance (mere resolution) and true repentance (with regret and sorrow), and identify the sign of genuine tawba.

Core Idea
True repentance requires heartfelt regret, not just a verbal commitment.

In everyday language, people often use the word 'tawba' loosely to mean simply deciding to stop sinning. But Imam Sanusi clarifies that this falls short of the Islamic concept of repentance. He insists that without genuine regret and sorrow over past sins, there is no real repentance, and he provides a clear sign of true remorse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details, then read the translation. Focus on the author's correction of popular misuse.
2
Identify the common misuse
Notice that Sanusi says common people apply 'tawba' to merely showing resolution to abandon sin in the future. Recognize that this is the error he corrects.
3
Learn the essential elements of true repentance
Sanusi states that true repentance requires regret (nadam) and sorrow (asaf) over past sins. Understand that these are internal states, not just outward statements.
Regret is the core of tawba in Islamic theology.
4
Understand the sign of true regret
The sign of genuine regret is prolonged grief and sorrow, and shedding tears. This indicates the depth of remorse, though it is not a mandatory ritual.
Tears are a natural expression of deep sorrow, not a requirement.
5
Reflect on personal application
Consider your own understanding of repentance. Does your repentance include heartfelt regret? Use this lesson to deepen your practice of tawba.

The Rare Virtue of True Repentance
Sanusi emphasizes the rarity of true repentance, citing al-Ghazali and David's story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why true repentance is considered extremely rare and difficult in Islamic theology, and how the example of Prophet David and al-Ghazali's teachings illustrate this.

Core Idea
True repentance is a rarity, attained only by the exceptional few.

Sanusi draws attention to a sobering truth: genuine repentance is not a casual act but a profound spiritual transformation. He directs us to two authoritative sources—al-Ghazali's Ihya' and the story of Prophet David's istighfar—to show that only the rarest individuals achieve true tawba. This lesson unpacks that challenge and the hope encapsulated in Sanusi's closing supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation

3
Grasp the central claim

4
Reflect on the supplication
See how Sanusi ends with a plea for sincere repentance. This shows that true repentance is both a goal and a divine gift.

When Repentance Must Be Renewed
Sanusi states the rule for renewing tawba when recalling a sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which renewal of repentance becomes obligatory according to Sanusi, and how this view differs from that of al-Qadi and Abu Ali.

Core Idea
Repentance need not be renewed unless recalling the sin brings desire or joy.

Sanusi addresses a practical question: if a person remembers a past sin, must they renew their repentance? The answer is not automatic. This passage clarifies the intention behind the recall as the decisive factor, and contrasts Sanusi's position with that of two other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the ruling.
Notice the key words: mushtahiyan (desiring) and farihan (rejoicing).
2
Identify the condition
The passage states that renewal is required only when the sin is recalled with desire and joy. Note both conditions must be present.
3
Compare with contrary view
Al-Qadi and Abu Ali hold that renewal is required absolutely, without condition. Understand the difference.
4
Reflect on implication
Consider why the inner state matters: desire and joy indicate the repentance was insincere or broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم تجديد التوبة كلما ذكر المعصية، إلا أن يذكرها مشتهياً لها، فرحاً بها، خلافاً للقاضي منا، ولأبي علي من المعتزلة؛ فإنهما أوجبا تجديدها بعد ذكر المعصية مطلقاً.
And renewal of repentance is not required every time one mentions the sin, unless one mentions it desiring it, rejoicing in it, contrary to al-Qadi among us and to Abu Ali from the Mu'tazila; for they obligated its renewal after mentioning the sin absolutely.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on whether a person must renew their repentance every time they recall a past sin. The author states that renewal is not required in all cases. It is only required if, when recalling the sin, one feels desire for it or joy in it—indicating that the repentance may have been insincere or broken. The author notes that this view differs from two scholars: al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) from the Ash'ari school, and Abu Ali (al-Jubba'i) from the Mu'tazila, who both held that renewal is obligatory whenever the sin is remembered, without any condition. The passage thus limits the obligation to situations where the recollection involves sinful pleasure.

Key Terms
تجديد التوبة
ذكر المعصية
مشتهياً
فرحاً
القاضي
أبو علي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawba must be renewed every time the sin crosses one's mind.
Correction: This passage says it is only required when recalled with desire.

Checkpoint
Under what condition is renewal of repentance required upon remembering a sin?
A) Whenever the sin is remembered.
B) Only if the sin is remembered with desire and joy.
C) Only if the sin is committed again.
D) Renewal is never required.

Key Takeaway
Renewal of repentance is only obligatory when the sin is recalled with desire or joy, not merely on recollection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you remember a sin from your past, do you ever catch yourself smiling or feeling a sense of nostalgia? What does that moment reveal about the sincerity of your repentance to Allah? Will you now, in this moment, ask Allah to purify your heart from any lingering attachment to that sin?

Be honest: have you ever avoided renewing repentance because it would mean admitting your heart is still not clean?
?
Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) said, 'The one who repents from sin is like the one who has no sin.' But what if, deep down, you are secretly pleased that you once committed a sin because of the pleasure it gave you? How would you confront that hidden joy in front of Allah? Take a moment right now to confess any secret delight over past sins and ask for a heart that truly despises them.

Partial Repentance is Valid
Sanusi affirms partial repentance against Abu Hashim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari scholars hold that repentance from some sins while persisting in others is valid, contrary to the Mu'tazili view of Abu Hashim.

Core Idea
You can repent from one sin while still struggling with another.

This short but significant passage settles a key question about repentance: must it be comprehensive? Sanusi presents the Ash'ari position that repentance can be partial, using a clear analogy from obligatory and evil acts. Read on to see the reasoning and the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the explicit disagreement with Abu Hashim and the analogy introduced by 'our companions'.
The passage is compact: a claim, a refutation, and an analogy.
2
Understand the analogy
The analogy compares repenting from some sins to performing one obligatory act while neglecting another. Just as one can do a good deed despite failing at another, one can abandon an evil deed while still committing another.
The key is that motives for each act are independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح التوبة من بعض المعاصي دون بعض، خلافاً لأبي هاشم من المعتزلة، قال أصحابنا: إنه كما لا يجوز الإتيان بواجب لحسنه وقوة دواعيه مع ترك واجب آخر.. كذلك يجوز ترك قبيح لقبحه وضعف دواعي فعله مع الإصرار على قبيح آخر.
And repentance from some sins without others is valid, contrary to Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila. Our companions said: just as it is not permissible to perform an obligatory act due to its goodness and strength of its motives while abandoning another obligatory act, similarly it is permissible to abandon an evil act due to its ugliness and weakness of the motives for doing it while persisting in another evil act.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage deals with whether repentance must be from all sins simultaneously or can be from some while still committing others. The author states that repentance from some sins but not others is valid. This is contrary to the Mu'tazili scholar Abu Hashim, who allegedly required a comprehensive repentance. The author explains the reasoning of 'our companions' (the Ash'ari scholars): just as it is possible for a person to perform one obligatory act (because of its inherent goodness and strong motivation) while neglecting another obligatory act, similarly it is possible for a person to abandon one evil act (because of its ugliness and weak motivation) while still persisting in another evil act. The point is that the motives for each sin are independent, and genuine regret can target a specific sin even if other sins remain.

Key Terms
توبة (tawba)
بعض المعاصي (ba'd al-ma'asi)
أبو هاشم (Abu Hashim)
أصحابنا (ashabuna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance is invalid unless it covers all sins simultaneously.
Correction: Repentance from some sins only is valid, as independently motivated regret for a specific sin suffices.

Checkpoint

A) Performing one duty while neglecting another.
B) A sick person recovering gradually.
C) The impossibility of abandoning all sins.
D) The mercy of Allah outweighing all sins.

Key Takeaway
Repentance can be valid for individual sins even if other sins remain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The lesson says you cannot use one obedience to justify neglecting another, but you can repent from one sin while still struggling with others. The lesson's argument distinguishes between obligations and sins. But consider: have you ever tried to bargain with Allah by pointing to your obedience in one area to justify disobedience in another? What does this passage reveal about that attitude? How can you begin to see each sin as deserving of its own repentance, regardless of your other actions?

Sanusi rules that a general repentance covers all sins even if details are known.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A general repentance covers all sins; no need to list them.

Sanusi concludes his discussion on repentance with a practical and merciful ruling. You may wonder if you need to recall every sin you ever committed. This passage clarifies that a sincere, general repentance is enough, removing the burden of enumeration.

Guided Path

This connects to Islamic principles of removing hardship.

Renewing Tawba When Remembering Sin

By the end of this lesson, you will understand when renewing tawba is necessary upon recalling a past sin and how this differs from the views of al-Qadi and Abu Ali.

Core Idea
Not every memory of a past sin breaks repentance—only if it comes with desire or joy.

When we repent from a sin, are we required to renew that repentance every time the memory crosses our mind? The author addresses this practical question with a nuanced ruling: mere recollection does not invalidate tawba; it only becomes necessary if one recalls the sin with approval and delight. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this position and the contrast with stricter opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Validity of Partial Repentance
Sanusi defends partial repentance using an analogy with obligatory acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repentance from some sins is valid even if one continues committing other sins, based on the Ash'ari refutation of Abu Hashim.

Core Idea
Repentance can be piecemeal; each sin has its own motivation.

This lesson addresses a common question: does true repentance require turning away from all sins at once? Sanusi draws on an Ash'ari argument to show that repentance from individual sins is possible, using an analogy with obligatory acts to illustrate the independence of each sin's motivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the main claim and the refutation of Abu Hashim.
Notice the concise style; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila held that repentance from some sins while persisting in others is invalid. Contrast this with the Ash'ari position.
3
Trace the analogy
Sanusi draws an analogy: just as one may perform an obligatory act while leaving another (which is sinful but the act itself is valid), one may abandon an evil deed while persisting in another. Understand how the analogy supports the claim.
The analogy hinges on the idea that each act has its own motivation and moral evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح التوبةُ مِنْ بعض المعاصي دونَ بعض ، خلافاً لأبي هاشم مِنَ المعتزلة ، قال أصحابنا : إنَّهُ كما لا يجوزُ الإتيان بواجب لحُسْنِهِ وقوَّةِ دواعيه مع ترك واجب آخر .. كذلك يجوز تركُ قبيح لقبحِهِ وضعْفِ دواعي فعله مع الإصرار على قبيح آخر
And repentance from some sins is valid without [repentance from] others, contrary to Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila. Our companions said: Just as it is not permissible to perform an obligatory act due to its goodness and the strength of its motivations while abandoning another obligatory act, similarly it is permissible to abandon an evil act due to its evilness and the weakness of motivations for doing it while persisting in another evil act.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the validity of partial repentance—repenting from some sins while continuing in others. It refutes the Mu'tazili Abu Hashim, who required a comprehensive repentance. The argument uses an analogy: just as one may perform one obligatory act but leave another (which is sinful, but the act itself is valid), similarly one may abandon one evil deed out of recognition of its evil and lack of motivation, even if one persists in another evil. The key point is that each sin has its own motivation, and repentance is about turning from that specific sin.

أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance must cover all sins simultaneously; partial repentance is invalid.
Correction: Each sin has its own motivation; turning away from one sin out of recognition of its evil is valid, even if one persists in another sin.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to support the validity of repenting from some sins but not others?
A) Forgiving some debts and not others
B) Performing one obligatory act while leaving another
C) Fasting some days and not others
D) Praying in congregation vs. alone

Key Takeaway
Each sin is evaluated individually; repenting from one does not require repenting from all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a sin in your life that you have convinced yourself is 'small' or 'manageable,' while you take pride in avoiding more 'serious' sins? What does this passage teach you about the independence of each sin's motivation—and the honesty needed to address that sin you've been ignoring?
?
You have repented from some sins, but what are the ones you still cling to—and why? Can you name one evil deed you continue to commit, and trace it back to a motivation you haven't truly hated? What would it mean to turn from that sin not because of social pressure, but because you see its evil in itself?
?
How often do you use your partial repentance as a shield against the full transformation Allah calls for? What would change today if you treated each sin—even the 'small' ones—as a separate call to return to Allah, without waiting to have all your sins sorted at once?

General Repentance and Detailed Requirements
Explains sufficiency of general repentance and details requirements for sins against God and people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that general repentance suffices for all sins, but sins involving the rights of Allah or others may require additional concrete actions beyond remorse.

Core Idea
Repentance repairs your relationship with Allah; for wrongs against people, you must also repair the harm.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a key practical question: what exactly does repentance require? While some scholars demand detailed remorse for each sin, Sanusi affirms that general repentance is sufficient. However, when a sin involves the rights of another person, additional steps like restitution or apology become necessary—separate obligations that complement but are not part of repentance itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice the structure: general principle, then details for sins against God and people, then verification.
The passage includes quotes from Taftazani and Imam al-Haramayn.
2
Understand the general principle
Sanusi states that general repentance (al-ijmāl) is sufficient for all sins, even if known in detail, contrary to some Mu'tazila who require detailed remorse. This is a lenient view that simplifies repentance.
The principle applies whether the sin is against God or people.
3
Examine the requirements for sins against God
For pure rights of God (e.g., fleeing battle, neglecting commanding good), remorse may suffice. But some sins require extra actions like surrendering for punishment (drinking wine) or paying missed obligations (zakat, prayer).
The extra actions are obligations alongside repentance, not part of it.
4
Examine the requirements for sins against people
For oppression (usurpation, murder), restitution or surrender for retaliation is needed. For misguidance, guidance. For harm (backbiting), apology. Backbiting doesn't require detailing unless the victim heard a worse version.
Imam al-Haramayn distinguishes between murder (remorse suffices for God, but refusal to surrender is a new sin) and usurpation (remorse invalid while holding property).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكفي في التوبة عن المعاصي كلّها الإجمالُ وإِنْ عُلِمَتْ مفصلةً ، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة أنَّه لا بدَّ مِنَ الندم تفصيلاً فيما علم ) قالَ التفتازاني : ( قالوا : ثم إنْ كانَتِ المعصية في خالص حق الله تعالى . . فقد يكفي الندم ؛ كما في ارتكاب الفِرارِ مِنَ الزحف ، وتركِ الأمر بالمعروفِ ، وقد يحتاجُ إلى أمرِ زائد ؛ كتسليم النفس للحد في الشرب ، وتسليم ما وجبَ في تركِ الزكاة ، ومثله في ترك الصلاة وإن تعلَّقتْ بحقوق العبادِ .. لزم معَ الندم إيصال حق العبدِ أو بدلِهِ إليهِ إنْ كانَ الذنب ظلماً ؛ كما في الغصب والقتل العمد ، ولزم إرشادُهُ إِنْ كانَ الذنبُ إضلالاً له ، والاعتذار إليهِ إنْ كانَ إيذاءً كما في الغِيبةِ . ولا يلزم تفصيلُ ما اغتابَهُ بهِ إلا إذا بلغه على وجه أفحش ثم التحقيق : أنَّ هذا الزائد واجبٌ آخر خارج عن التوبةِ ، قالَ إمام الحرمين : « إِنَّ القاتل إذا ندمَ مِنْ غيرِ تسليم نفسِهِ للقصاص صحت توبته في حقٌّ الله تعالى ، وكانَ منعه للقصاصِ مِنْ مُستحقِّهِ معصيةً مُتجدّدةً تستدعي توبةً أخرى ، فلا تقدح في التوبة عن القتل » ، ثمَّ قال : « وربَّما لا تصح التوبة بدونِ الخروج مِنْ حقِّ العبدِ ، كما في الغصب ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يصح الندم عليهِ معَ إدامة اليد على المغصوب ، ففرق بينَ القتل والغصب »
And general [repentance] is sufficient for repentance from all sins, even if they are known in detail, contrary to some Mu'tazila who say that detailed remorse is necessary for what is known. Al-Taftazani said: They said: Then if the sin is in pure right of God, then remorse may suffice—as in committing flight from battle and abandoning commanding good—and it may need an additional matter, such as surrendering oneself for the hadd penalty in drinking wine, and surrendering what is obligatory in abandoning zakat, and similarly in abandoning prayer. And if it pertains to the rights of people, then along with remorse it is necessary to deliver the right of the servant or its substitute to him if the sin is oppression, as in usurpation and intentional murder; and it is necessary to guide him if the sin is leading him astray, and to apologize to him if it is harm, as in backbiting. And it is not necessary to detail what one backbited about except if it reached him in a more severe manner. Then the verification: that this additional [matter] is another obligation outside of repentance. Imam al-Haramayn said: Indeed, if the murderer regrets without surrendering himself for retaliation, his repentance is valid in the right of God, and his prevention of retaliation from its claimant is a renewed sin that requires another repentance, so it does not invalidate [the] repentance from murder. Then he said: And perhaps repentance is not valid without exiting from the right of the servant, as in usurpation; for indeed, regret is not valid while continuing to hold the usurped property, so there is a difference between murder and usurpation.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage addresses two main issues. First, it affirms that a general, undetailed repentance suffices for all sins, contrary to some Mu'tazila who demand detailed remorse. Then it discusses the specific requirements when the sin involves rights of God or rights of people. For sins against God (like fleeing battle or neglecting commanding good), remorse alone may suffice, but sometimes extra actions are needed (e.g., submitting to punishment for drinking wine, paying missed zakat or prayer). For sins against people (oppression, misguidance, harm), additional steps are required: restitution, guidance, or apology. Backbiting does not require detailing the content unless it reached the person in a worse form. Finally, the verification (تحقيق) clarifies that these extra actions are separate obligations, not part of repentance itself. Imam al-Haramayn distinguishes between murder (where repentance is valid without surrendering for retaliation, but the refusal to surrender is a new sin) and usurpation (where repentance requires returning the property). This shows nuanced fiqh of tawba.

Key Terms
الإجمال
التفصيل
حق الله
حق العبد
الندم
إمام الحرمين
تفتازاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'I repent' is enough to cancel all consequences of sin, even those involving other people's rights.
Correction: While general repentance suffices for God's right, sins against people require concrete actions like returning property, apologizing, or guiding them. Without these, repentance is incomplete.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, what is the difference between repentance from murder and repentance from usurpation?
A) Murder requires giving blood money, usurpation does not
B) Repentance from murder is valid without surrendering for retaliation, but usurpation requires returning the property
C) Both require the same steps
D) Usurpation cannot be forgiven

Key Takeaway
General repentance suffices for all sins, but sins against people require additional concrete obligations that are separate from the act of tawba.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person you have wronged—whether by taking something, harming their reputation, or leading them astray. Have you sincerely tried to make it right? If not, what is stopping you from taking that step today? Are you willing to humble yourself and seek their forgiveness, even if it costs you your pride?
?
When you repent for a sin that involved only Allah, is your remorse genuine, or do you expect that a simple 'Astaghfirullah' wipes the slate clean without any change in your actions? Are you willing to take the extra step—like making up missed prayers or giving in charity—to prove your repentance is real?
?
If you were to die tonight, would your repentance be complete? Is there any right of another person that you have not returned, any apology you have not made, or any harm you have not repaired? What would it take for you to leave this world with a clean slate?

Repentance from Sins Against Allah: Regret and Additional Acts
Sanusi outlines the conditions for repentance regarding sins against Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for repentance when the sin is purely against Allah, and how regret may be sufficient or require additional actions.

Core Idea
Repentance from sins against Allah often only requires regret, but sometimes needs extra acts.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the foundational role of regret in repentance. However, the requirements differ based on the type of sin. This lesson focuses on sins that are purely against Allah, where regret may be enough, but sometimes additional actions like surrendering for punishment are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the Mu'tazila view before stating his own.
The passage contrasts with some Mu'tazila who require detailed regret.
2
Identify the two subcategories
Within sins against Allah, find the two cases: where regret suffices and where an additional matter is needed. Note the examples given.
Examples: fleeing battle (regret suffices), drinking alcohol (need to surrender for punishment).
3
Understand the principle
The core idea is that regret is essential, but for some sins against Allah, an extra action is required to complete repentance.
This sets the stage for the more complex discussion of sins against humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة أنَّه لا بدَّ مِنَ الندم تفصيلاً فيما علم ) قالَ التفتازاني : ( قالوا : ثم إنْ كانَتِ المعصية في خالص حق الله تعالى . . فقد يكفي الندم ؛ كما في ارتكاب الفِرارِ مِنَ الزحف ، وتركِ الأمر بالمعروفِ ، وقد يحتاجُ إلى أمرِ زائد ؛ كتسليم النفس للحد في الشرب ، وتسليم ما وجبَ في تركِ الزكاة ، ومثله في ترك الصلاة( وإن تعلَّقتْ بحقوق العبادِ .. لزم معَ الندم إيصال حق العبدِ أو بدلِهِ إليهِ إنْ كانَ الذنب ظلماً ؛ كما في الغصب والقتل العمد ، ولزم إرشادُهُ إِنْ كانَ الذنبُ إضلالاً له ، والاعتذار إليهِ إنْ كانَ إيذاءً كما في الغِيبةِ . ولا يلزم تفصيلُ ما اغتابَهُ بهِ إلا إذا بلغه على وجه أفحش ثم التحقيق : أنَّ هذا الزائد واجبٌ آخر خارج عن التوبةِ ، قالَ إمام الحرمين : « إِنَّ القاتل إذا ندمَ مِنْ غيرِ تسليم نفسِهِ للقصاص صحت توبته في حقٌّ الله تعالى ، وكانَ منعه للقصاصِ مِنْ مُستحقِّهِ معصيةً مُتجدّدةً تستدعي توبةً أخرى ، فلا تقدح في التوبة عن القتل ، ثمَّ قال : « وربَّما لا تصح التوبة بدونِ الخروج مِنْ حقِّ العبدِ ، كما في الغصب ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يصح الندم عليهِ معَ إدامة اليد على المغصوب ، ففرق بينَ القتل والغصب » ( انتهى
Contrary to some Mu'tazila that detailed regret is necessary for what one knows. Al-Taftazani said: They said: Then if the sin is purely concerning the right of Allah the Exalted, regret may suffice, as in committing flight from battle and abandoning commanding good, and it may need an additional matter, such as surrendering oneself for the prescribed punishment in drinking, and surrendering what was obligated in abandoning zakat, and similarly in abandoning prayer. And if it pertains to the rights of servants, then along with regret it is necessary to deliver the right of the servant or its substitute to him if the sin is oppression, as in usurpation and intentional murder; and it is necessary to guide him if the sin is misguidance, and to apologize to him if it is harm, as in backbiting. And detailed disclosure of what one backbited him with is not necessary unless it reached him in a more heinous manner. Then the verification: this additional matter is another obligation outside of repentance. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Indeed, if the killer regrets without surrendering himself for retaliation, his repentance is valid with respect to the right of Allah the Exalted, and his preventing retaliation from its rightful owner is a renewed sin that requires another repentance, so it does not impair repentance from murder.' Then he said: 'And perhaps repentance is not valid without exiting from the right of the servant, as in usurpation; for regret over it is not valid while the hand continues to hold the usurped property, so there is a difference between murder and usurpation.' (End)
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the conditions for valid repentance. It begins by contrasting the view of some Mu'tazila who require detailed regret for every sin. Al-Taftazani is then cited, explaining that if a sin is purely against Allah's rights (e.g., fleeing battle, abandoning commanding good, or skipping prayer), regret may suffice, but sometimes an additional action is needed, like surrendering for punishment. If the sin involves rights of people, then alongside regret, one must either deliver the right or its substitute (if oppression), guide the person (if misguidance), or apologize (if harm, like backbiting). However, detailing the backbiting is not required unless the victim heard it in a worse form. Then the author gives the verification: this extra requirement is a separate obligation outside repentance. He quotes Imam al-Haramayn, who distinguishes murder from usurpation: in murder, if the killer regrets but does not surrender for retaliation, his repentance to Allah is valid, but withholding retaliation is a new sin needing separate repentance. In usurpation, repentance is invalid without returning the stolen item because one cannot genuinely regret while still holding it. Thus, the key point is that repentance from sins against humans often requires restoring rights, while sins against Allah may only require regret plus any necessary acts of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says that for some sins, regret alone is not enough—you must surrender to the consequence or fulfill the obligation. Is there a sin in your life where you have avoided the required restitution because it would be costly or humbling? When will you take that step?

Repentance from Sins Against Humans: Restoring Rights
Sanusi enumerates the requirements for repentance regarding sins against human rights with a verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional requirements for repentance when sins involve the rights of others, and the distinction between murder and usurpation.

Core Idea
Sins against humans require regret plus restoring rights, guidance, or apology—and the obligation is separate from repentance.

Sanusi now turns to sins that involve the rights of other people. He explains that in addition to regret, one must fulfill the affected person's rights: deliver what is owed, guide if misled, or apologize if harmed. He concludes with a verification by Imam al-Haramayn clarifying that these extra actions are separate obligations, and distinguishing between murder and usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for sins against humans
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the three categories: oppression (requires delivering right or substitute), misguidance (requires guiding), and harm (requires apology).
Also notice the special case of backbiting.
2
Understand the verification
The passage then states that this extra requirement is a separate obligation outside repentance. Imam al-Haramayn's quote illustrates the distinction between murder and usurpation.
In murder, regret can be valid without surrendering for retaliation, but usurpation requires returning the property.
3
Apply the principle
Reflect on why the distinction exists: in usurpation, the sin continues as long as one holds the property, while in murder, the act is past.
This helps understand the nature of ongoing sins.

Think of a specific person you have wronged by oppression (taking something, hurting them) and only felt sorry without making it right. Based on this lesson, what is the status of your repentance? What concrete step will you take this week to restore their right or apologize?
?
You have backbitten someone and never apologized because it would be awkward. This lesson says apology is required unless the victim heard it in a worse form. Is your silence protecting their feelings or your ego? Will you let the sun set before you humbly seek their forgiveness?
?
Is there someone you have led astray or influenced negatively, even unintentionally? This lesson commands you to guide them back. How can you repair that damage without excusing yourself? What is stopping you from reaching out today?
